[INFERENCE: the chapter's framing of Watkins's argument as primarily a *philological* inheritance argument — distinct from structural-typological or theological inheritance arguments — is the chapter's editorial reading of the methodological framing chapters of Watkins 1995, presented in the present chapter as the principal interpretive lens for §§II–IV; Watkins's volume is itself titled *Aspects of Indo-European Poetics* and the verbal-formulaic emphasis is consonant with the volume's self-presentation, but the framing language used in the present chapter ("philological-genealogical inheritance distinct from structural-typological resemblance") is a chapter-level synthesis rather than a Watkins-1995 quotation. | basis: Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters and the volume's title ("Aspects of Indo-European Poetics"); brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml comparative_argument on the chapter's framing of the formula as IE poetic inheritance; per the chapter-claims skill discipline that requires editorial framing-language to be flagged where it tightens or interprets a source's argument | risk: alternative readings of Watkins's volume could foreground the structural or the typological dimensions; the chapter's philological-emphasis framing is one reasonable reading but not the only available reading.]

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch14 (Indra-Vṛtra) — back-link to the Vedic-side anchor (Jamison & Brereton 2014 on RV 1.32 and the supplementary hymn-cycle; Benveniste & Renou 1934 on the Vedic-Avestan philological core); §I above introduces the Vedic *áhann áhim* phrasing as the formula's reflex anchor and §II below develops the Vedic case in more detail.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch6 (Illuyanka) — back-link to the Hittite-side anchor (Hoffner 1998 working English translation of CTH 321; Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25 *editio princeps*); the Watkins formula material is treated more briefly in Ch6 (which is principally a *purulli*-festival chapter — see Ch36 §III) and the present chapter's §II develops the Hittite philological case as one of the three direct cases.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch33 (Methods) — back-link to the methods sidebar's discussion of philological-comparative method, in which Watkins's lexical-syntactic-correspondence method is contrasted with structural-typological and functional-comparative methods; the present chapter's §I framing of the formula as philological-genealogical inheritance is the comparative-chapter realisation of the methods sidebar's distinctions.


=== II. The direct philological cases

The §II direct philological cases are the three IE daughter-language reflexes — Vedic Indra-Vṛtra, Avestan Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka, and Hittite Tarḫunna-Illuyanka — at which Watkins's reconstructed formula is anchored on regular lexical-and-syntactic correspondences across attested-text witnesses, and at which the chapter's case for Indo-European inheritance does not depend on typological reading or on reconstruction from indirect evidence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the Vedic-Avestan-Hittite triple as the philologically-direct anchor of the reconstructed formula; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section II on the three-direct-case framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (Vedic / Avestan / Hittite as direct anchor)

The Vedic case is anchored on Rigveda 1.32, the principal Indra-Vṛtra hymn (fifteen verses), whose second verse contains *áhann áhim*, "he slew the serpent" — the formula's most-cited lexical exhibit; the chapter cites RV 1.32 as the working text via Jamison and Brereton 2014, the scholarly-standard English Rigveda, and reads the *áhann áhim* phrase at 1.32.2 as the Vedic instantiation of the verb-and-object pairing that Watkins reconstructs as the formula.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on RV 1.32 as the principal Indra-Vṛtra hymn and on the *áhann áhim* phrasing at 1.32.2 (the second verse, with the opening verse announcing the theme of Indra's manly deeds); Watkins 1995, ch. 46 (and broader Part V), on the same line as the Vedic reflex anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 1.32 (and 1.32.1-2)

The Vedic Vṛtra-corpus extends well beyond RV 1.32: at least eight Rigvedic hymns (RV 1.32; 1.80; 2.11; 2.12; 4.18; 5.32; 6.17; 8.96; 10.113) constitute the working Vṛtra-combat corpus on which Watkins's reading of the Vedic reflex draws, with RV 1.32 supplying the most concentrated narrative spine and the other seven hymns supplying co-witness motifs that the spine-text does not develop, and the formula recurs across the corpus rather than being a one-line feature of a single hymn.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the eight Vṛtra-hymns as the working Vṛtra-combat corpus; brief 14-indra-vrtra.yaml sources.primary on the same eight hymns; Watkins 1995, chs. 41–48, on the recurrence of the formula across the Vṛtra-corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 1.32; 1.80; 2.11-12; 4.18; 5.32; 6.17; 8.96; 10.113
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=chs. 41-48 (Vedic Vṛtra-corpus)

The Vedic combat is liturgically embedded: the Rigveda is a corpus of ritual hymns recited at the *soma* sacrifice, and the *áhann áhim* phrasing appears in hymns whose ritual function is to invoke the *soma*-strengthened Indra in the present petition, so that every recitation of RV 1.32 in its ritual context re-actualises the formula in performance — a feature that distinguishes the Vedic reflex from the Hittite reflex (where the formula appears in a one-time CTH 321 narrative rather than in a daily-or-frequent ritual hymn).footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the Rigveda's liturgical character and on the ritual-recitation context of the Vṛtra-hymns; per brief 14-indra-vrtra.yaml on the *soma*-sacrifice context of the Vṛtra-hymns; Watkins 1995, on the Vedic reflex's poetic-liturgical character.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=Vṛtra-hymns (liturgical-ritual context)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Vedic poetic-liturgical context)

The Vedic reflex is reinforced by Trita Āptya, the older Vedic dragon-slayer whose name (Vedic *Trita*, "third") is philologically cognate with Avestan Thraētaona by regular Indo-Iranian sound-laws (Vedic *tr-* / Avestan *thr-*; Vedic *-i-* / Avestan *-aē-*; Vedic *-a* / Avestan *-aona*), and whose attestation in the Rigvedic corpus (RV 10.99.6; 1.105; 2.11.19–20) preserves a pre-Indra Indo-Iranian stratum of the dragon-slayer pattern.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Trita / Thraētaona phonological cognancy and on the pre-Indra Indo-Iranian stratum; Jamison and Brereton 2014, on RV 10.99.6 and 1.105 and 2.11.19–20 as the Trita Āptya hymn-cluster; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–45, on the Trita / Thraētaona pair as a load-bearing Indo-Iranian exhibit.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita-Thraētaona discussion
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 10.99.6; 1.105; 2.11.19-20

The Trita / Thraētaona cognancy is documented at two levels — at the level of the names themselves (the regular sound-law correspondences across the Indo-Iranian branches) and at the level of their adversaries (both figures slay a three-headed serpent, the Vedic adversary unnamed in the surviving hymn-allusions and the Avestan adversary named as Aži Dahāka, "the dragon-Dahāka") — and the pair is one of the load-bearing exhibits for an Indo-Iranian phase of the dragon-combat material before the divergence of the Indic and Iranian branches.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the lexical-and-structural Trita / Thraētaona cognancy as evidence for an Indo-Iranian stratum; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–45, on the same evidential function; per brief 15-vedic-reflexes.yaml on the philologically-load-bearing status of the pair.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita-Thraētaona discussion (Indo-Iranian phase)

The Avestan case is anchored on the Avestan yashts (Yt. 5.33–35, in which Thraētaona prays to Anāhitā for victory over Aži Dahāka; Yt. 14, in which Vərəθraγna's smashing of resistance is named; Yt. 19, the Frashokereti material on the eschatological combat) and on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn*, in which the Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka combat is preserved as a continuous narrative; Benveniste and Renou 1934 supply the standard philological apparatus integrating both layers.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Avestan yasht material (Yt. 5.33–35; Yt. 14; Yt. 19) and on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* narrative; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–48, on the Avestan reflexes within the consolidated IE formula reconstruction; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml on the Avestan-and-Pahlavi witness layers.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Yt. 5.33-35; Yt. 14; Yt. 19; Bundahišn material
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V chs. 44-48 (Avestan reflexes)

The Avestan reflex is hymn-allusive rather than narrative: the surviving Avestan corpus does not preserve a continuous narrative of the Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka combat, and the yasht-allusions — Yt. 5.33–35 in particular, where Thraētaona prays for the strength to slay the three-mouthed, three-headed, six-eyed Aži Dahāka — supply only the verbal-and-prayer-context attestations of the combat at the level of formulaic invocation rather than at the level of full storytelling.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the hymn-allusive character of the Avestan attestations and on Yt. 5.33–35 specifically; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae on the absence of a continuous Avestan narrative and on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* as the fullest narrative witness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Yt. 5.33-35 (hymn-allusive prayer-context)

The Pahlavi *Bundahišn* — a Middle Persian compilation conventionally dated to the 9th century CE in its surviving redaction, drawing on earlier Sasanian-period materials — supplies the fullest narrative witness to the Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka combat (the binding of the dragon under Mount Damāvand) and to the eschatological pendant in which Aži Dahāka escapes and is finally killed by the revived Kərəsāspa at Frashokereti; the Pahlavi witness is post-Achaemenid and must be marked as such in any Indo-European-comparative argument that draws on it.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* as the fullest narrative witness and on the binding-and-final-killing structure; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–48, on the Pahlavi material's use within the consolidated IE reconstruction; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae on the post-Achaemenid status of the Pahlavi witness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Pahlavi Bundahišn (continuous narrative witness)

[LACUNA: the Avestan corpus preserves no continuous narrative of the Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka combat; the yasht-allusions are hymn-formulaic-and-prayer-context, and the continuous-narrative witness is the post-Achaemenid Pahlavi *Bundahišn*. | source: per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae and per the Avestan yasht corpus as preserved in standard editions; Benveniste and Renou 1934 on the hymn-allusive character of the Avestan attestations | scholarly_reconstruction: Benveniste and Renou 1934 reconstruct the combat-narrative by reading the Avestan yasht-allusions alongside the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* narrative, with the Pahlavi-witness narrative read as the continuation-and-elaboration of the Avestan-attested combat-tradition; the chapter cites the Pahlavi material as the in-tradition fill rather than as an Avestan-period attestation.]

The Avestan adversary Aži Dahāka is morphologically three-headed (Avestan: three-mouthed, six-eyed, three-jawed), and the three-headed serpent / dragon morphology is precisely the morphology of the Vedic adversary slain by Trita Āptya in RV 10.99.6 — read by Benveniste and Renou and by Watkins as the Indo-Iranian common adversary of the Trita / Thraētaona cognate pair, with the cognancy of the names confirming the cognancy of the combat-narratives.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the three-headed-adversary cognancy across Trita / Aži Dahāka and Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–45, on the structural-and-philological cognancy of the Indo-Iranian dragon-slayer pair; Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the three-headed-adversary attestation at RV 10.99.6.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita-Thraētaona / three-headed-adversary discussion
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 10.99.6 (three-headed adversary)

The Hittite case — the chapter renders the storm-god as Tarḫunna in keeping with the Ch36 §III correction, since Tarḫunna is the Hittite-language name of the storm-god in CTH 321 and Teshub is the Hurrian-language name attested in the Kumarbi Cycle and Hurrian-language ritual material — is anchored on CTH 321 (the Illuyanka tablets from the Hattusa archives), preserved in two co-equal scribal versions on the same tablet group, with both versions framed by their colophons as the recitation belonging to the *purulli* festival of the storm-god of Nerik.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, *Hittite Myths*, "Illuyanka" §§1–2 with framing colophons; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25, on the *editio princeps* and the two-version structure; per glossary.yaml on "Tarḫunna / Teshub" with Tarḫunna in Hittite-language contexts and Teshub in Hurrian-language contexts.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2 with framing colophons
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25

The Hittite verb-and-object pairing *kuenta… illuyanka-* — "he slew… the serpent" — is the chapter's principal Hittite philological exhibit: the verb *kuen-* (third-singular preterite *kuenta*) is the Hittite reflex of the PIE killing-verb *gʷhen-* whose Vedic and Avestan reflexes (*han-* / *jan-*) anchor the formula in Indo-Iranian, and *illuyanka-* is the Hittite common-noun "serpent" used as the proper name of the storm-god's adversary.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 ("Typhoeus and the Illuyankas"), on the *kuenta… illuyanka-* verb-and-object pairing as the Hittite reflex of the IE formula; Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2, on the Hittite text containing the pairing; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25, on the *editio princeps*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite cognate formulation)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2

The two Illuyanka versions are co-equally attested on the same CTH 321 tablet group: Version 1 (Inara prepares a banquet, Hupasiya binds the gorged serpent, the storm-god kills the bound Illuyanka) and Version 2 (Illuyanka takes the storm-god's heart and eyes, the storm-god's half-mortal son recovers the organs as bride-price, the storm-god kills both Illuyanka and his own son in renewed combat at the sea); Beckman's *editio princeps* establishes the two versions as scribally co-equal, and the chapter cites both as carrying the Hittite philological reflex of the formula at the level of the verb-and-object pairing whose common cross-version status reinforces the philological signal.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2 on the two versions; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25 on the scribal co-equality of the two versions; per brief 06-illuyanka.yaml variants.classification on co-equal status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2 (two co-equal versions)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25 (scribal co-equality)

[LACUNA: the precise distribution of the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing across the two CTH 321 versions is not fully reconstructable in every preserved instance because the Hittite tablets themselves are partially broken (Version 1 closing section partially broken; Version 2 mid-narrative transitions partially broken per Hoffner 1998). | source: per brief 06-illuyanka.yaml lacunae on the broken closing section of Version 1 and the broken mid-narrative transitions of Version 2; Hoffner 1998 *Hittite Myths* on the textual breaks | scholarly_reconstruction: Watkins 1995 ch. 46 reads the formula as preserved in CTH 321 at the level of the verb-and-object pairing without depending on the breaks; Hoffner 1998 supplies the reconstructed text at the level of the surrounding narrative, and Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25 supplies the philological *editio princeps* against which the formula's Hittite-language realisations are checked.]

The Hittite reflex's chronological significance for the reconstruction is decisive: Hittite is conventionally regarded as an early-branching member of the IE family (the "Anatolian hypothesis" reading Anatolian as the first branch to separate; the "Indo-Hittite hypothesis" reading Anatolian as a sister-language to the rest of IE rather than a daughter), and the presence of the formula in CTH 321 alongside its Vedic and Avestan reflexes implies that the formula was already established at a stage of the family that predates the Anatolian / non-Anatolian divergence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 and methodological framing chapters, on the Hittite reflex's chronological significance; on the Anatolian-early-branching status of Hittite within the IE family; per glossary.yaml on "Proto-Indo-European" as the reconstructed common ancestor c. 4500–2500 BCE.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite chronological significance)

The three direct cases together — Vedic, Avestan, Hittite — supply philological evidence at three independent points in the IE family: Indo-Iranian (Vedic and Avestan as cognate witnesses to a shared Indo-Iranian phase of the formula) and Anatolian (Hittite as an independent early-branching witness), with the three-witness pattern crossing the Anatolian / non-Anatolian boundary and the Indo-Iranian internal boundary so that the formula's Indo-European depth is attested rather than reconstructed across at least two principal branches of the family.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the three-witness Vedic-Avestan-Hittite cross-branch attestation as the chapter's philological-evidential floor; Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Indo-Iranian internal-branch witnessing; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section II on the Vedic / Avestan / Hittite triple as the direct philological case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (cross-branch attestation)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Indo-Iranian internal-branch witnessing

The §II direct-cases argument's load-bearing claim is therefore that the formula's existence as an Indo-European inheritance is attested in three IE daughter languages on the strength of regular phonological correspondences between cognate verbs and cognate nouns, that the inheritance reading does not depend on speculative reconstruction or on typological resemblance, and that the §III extended cases — to which the chapter now turns — are best read as later-branching reflexes of the same inheritance whose evidential character varies along the gradient that §I and §IV develop.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the philological-attestation status of the §II direct cases and on the extended-cases gradient; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section II / III / IV on the three-direct-case status and on the gradient framing of the extended cases; per the Ch34 / Ch35 comparative-chapter precedent on the load-bearing-claim framing for §II material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (load-bearing direct-cases claim)

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch14 (Indra-Vṛtra) and Ch15 (Vedic reflexes) — back-link to the Vedic-side anchors (Jamison & Brereton 2014 on RV 1.32 and the supplementary hymn-cycle; Benveniste & Renou 1934 on the Trita / Thraētaona Indo-Iranian pair); §II above develops the Vedic case as the formula's most-cited reflex anchor and the Trita Āptya material as the pre-Indra Indo-Iranian stratum within the Vedic-side evidence.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch13 (Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka) — back-link to the Avestan-side anchor (Benveniste & Renou 1934 on the Avestan yashts and the Pahlavi *Bundahišn*; Watkins 1995 Part V chs. 44–48 on the Avestan reflexes); §II above develops the Avestan case with explicit hymn-allusive vs. continuous-narrative distinction between the Avestan and Pahlavi witnesses, and flags the Pahlavi-narrative status as post-Achaemenid in-tradition fill.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch6 (Illuyanka) and Ch36 §III (cyclical-maintenance) — back-link to the Hittite-side anchors (Hoffner 1998 *Hittite Myths* working English translation of CTH 321; Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25 *editio princeps*; Watkins 1995 ch. 46 on the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing); the chapter renders the storm-god as Tarḫunna in keeping with the Ch36 §III locked correction (Hittite-language storm-god of CTH 321) rather than as Teshub (the Hurrian-language form attested in the Kumarbi Cycle).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C4. Eschatological displacement (Ch37) — the Avestan / Pahlavi Frashokereti material on Aži Dahāka's binding-and-final-killing is treated at length in C4 as the eschatological-subtype Iranian case; the present chapter's §II treatment of the Avestan witness is restricted to the philological reflex of the formula at the level of the verb-and-object pairing and does not develop the eschatological reading that C4 develops.


=== III. The extended typological cases

The §III extended typological cases are the IE daughter-language reflexes — Greek Zeus-Typhon; Norse Thor-Jörmungandr and Sigurd-Fáfnir; Old English *Beowulf*-dragon; Celtic Lugh-Balor; Welsh Red-and-White Dragon; Ossetic Batraz; Armenian Vahagn-vishaps; Slavic Perun-Veles and Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych — at which Watkins's reconstructed formula is anchored in some cases on preserved poetic-formulaic diction (Norse Sigurd; Old English *Beowulf*) and in other cases on structural-typological resemblance, fragmentary attestation, or contested reconstruction; the §III cases are arranged here on a philological-evidence spectrum from strongest (Norse skaldic and Eddic; *Beowulf*) to weakest (Slavic Perun-Veles, Armenian Vahagn).footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the §III extended-cases gradient; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section III on the Greek / Norse / Germanic / Celtic / Welsh / Ossetic / Armenian / Slavic case-list; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the philological-evidence-spectrum ordering of the family-chapter sub-cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (extended-cases gradient)

The Greek Zeus-Typhon combat is the Hellenic instance of the IE warrior-and-serpent pattern: at Hesiod's *Theogony* 820–880 (the chapter's working text via West 1966), Zeus defeats the hundred-headed, serpent-limbed Typhon, the earth-born monster generated by Gaia and Tartarus, in the closing combat of the Olympian succession; the Typhonomachy is structurally the Greek sovereignty-establishing combat that secures Zeus's kingship over gods and men and is the principal Greek exhibit for the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern in Hesiodic poetic-narrative form.footnote:[West 1966, *Hesiod: Theogony*, on Theog. 820–880 as the closing combat of the Olympian succession and on the structural function of the Typhonomachy; Ogden 2013, *Drakōn*, chs. 2–3, on the Greek combat-tradition and on Typhon as the principal Greek instance; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the Hesiod-and-later-tradition framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1966-theogony ; loc=Theog. 820-880
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=chs. 2-3

The Greek-Hittite philological connection in the Zeus-Typhon material is contested rather than consolidated: West 1997 and Burkert 1992 read the Hesiodic succession (Ouranos-Kronos-Zeus) and the Typhonomachy as derived from the Kumarbi Cycle and (less directly) from the Illuyanka tradition via Near Eastern mediation, but this transmission-reading is the C6 chapter's territory rather than the present chapter's, and the present chapter treats Zeus-Typhon as a typological IE instance whose relation to the Hittite material is inheritance-or-contact rather than a direct philological cognate of the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing.footnote:[West 1997, *East Face of Helicon*, on the Kumarbi-Hesiod transmission reading; Burkert 1992, *Orientalizing Revolution*, on the Levantine transmission of combat-myth into Greek; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the Near-Eastern-transmission framing; per the C6 (Ch39) chapter on the transmission question that the present chapter does not adjudicate.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=East Face of Helicon (Kumarbi-Hesiod transmission)

The Norse case has two principal sub-instances within the IE-formula argument. The first is Thor against Jörmungandr — the world-encircling Midgard Serpent — preserved in three encounters across the Old Norse corpus (Útgarðaloki's lifting-of-the-cat in Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 46–48; the fishing expedition with Hymir in *Hymiskviða* and Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 48 with Húsdrápa as skaldic witness; the mutual killing at Ragnarök in *Völuspá* 56 and Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 51), and Watkins reads the skaldic kennings for the serpent (*umbgjǫrð allra landa* and similar phrases) as preserving the IE serpent-combat vocabulary at the level of the registered-poetic-diction kenning.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *The Poetic Edda*, on *Hymiskviða* and *Völuspá* 56; Faulkes 1987, on *Gylfaginning* 34, 46–48, and 51 and on the Húsdrápa skaldic citations; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the skaldic kennings and on the IE serpent-combat vocabulary; per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the three-encounter structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Hymiskviða; Völuspá 56
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 34, 46-48, 51
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Norse skaldic kennings)

The second Norse sub-instance is Sigurd against Fáfnir, preserved principally in *Fáfnismál* in the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE), with parallel witnesses in the *Völsunga saga* (c. 1270 CE) and in the Middle High German *Nibelungenlied* (c. 1200 CE) as the related Germanic witness with Sigurd in the German form Siegfried; Watkins's chapter 42 on Sigurd is among the strongest philological cases in the post-Indo-Iranian, post-Anatolian corpus, with the Old Norse verb of killing (*vega*, "to kill in combat") and the named hero-and-named-dragon pairing instantiating the IE formula in Old Norse.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems; Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the *Fáfnismál* diction of Sigurd's killing as a direct reflex of the reconstructed IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on Sigurd as the strongest philological evidence in the family chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42 (Sigurd / Fáfnir formula reflex)

The Old English *Beowulf* preserves the formula in the third movement of the poem (lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering), in which the aged Beowulf confronts and kills the treasure-hoarding *draca* and dies of the dragon's bite; Watkins reads the Old English dragon-vocabulary (*draca*, *wyrm*) and the killing-vocabulary as instantiating the IE formula in Old English, and Tolkien's 1936 British Academy lecture supplies the load-bearing critical move that re-established the mythic register of the *Beowulf* monsters within twentieth-century Old English scholarship.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the *Beowulf* dragon-episode as IE HERO SLAY SERPENT reflex in the killing-diction and the narrative-structure; Tolkien, J.R.R., "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics", *Proc. British Academy* 22 (1936): 245–295, on the dragon as proper mythic adversary integral to the poem's structure; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the convergence of the philological and the structural-critical arguments.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Beowulf formula reflex)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proc. British Academy 22 (1936)

The *Beowulf* case is also distinctive in that the dragon-vocabulary itself shows IE depth: the Old English *draca* (loaned from Latin *draco* but ultimately tracing to the same Greek-and-Indo-European stem from which Greek *drakōn* derives) and the inherited Germanic *wyrm* (from Proto-Germanic *wurmiz*, ultimately from PIE *wr̥mi-* "worm/serpent") supply two etymologically-distinct lexical exhibits for the serpent-object slot of the IE formula in a single Old English poem, and the doubled-vocabulary attestation reinforces the IE reflex-reading at the level of inherited word-stock rather than only at the level of preserved formulaic diction.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the Old English *draca* / *wyrm* doubled-vocabulary attestation as IE-formula reflex; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the *draca* / *wyrm* lexical pairing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (draca / wyrm doubled vocabulary)

The Celtic case is Lugh against Balor at the Second Battle of Mag Tuired, preserved in the Middle Irish prose narrative *Cath Maige Tuired* (manuscripts 12th–16th c. CE): Lugh of the Tuatha Dé Danann kills his evil-eyed Fomorian grandfather Balor with a sling-stone that drives the eye through the back of Balor's head and turns its destructive gaze on the Fomorian host behind him; West 2007 ch. 6 reads the Celtic case as the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern, with the morphological distinctiveness preserved (Balor is a one-eyed giant rather than a serpent, and the killing-weapon is a projectile rather than a sword or thunderbolt).footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Celtic Lugh-Balor case as the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern with morphological distinctiveness; Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the Cath Maige Tuired tradition; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Lugh-Balor sub-case; *Cath Maige Tuired* edition (Gray 1982 Irish Texts Society) named-but-flagged for inventory-audit follow-up at glossary-lock pinning.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Celtic Lugh-Balor)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The Lugh-Balor case's evidential weight for the IE formula argument is therefore principally typological and dynastic-structural: Lugh's hybrid lineage (Tuatha Dé father / Fomorian mother / Fomorian grandfather Balor) supplies the kill-the-grandfather motif that West 2007 ch. 6 reads as the IE warrior structural-genealogical pattern, but the philological reflex of *gʷhen-* in the Old Irish killing-vocabulary is not the focal exhibit, and the case's contribution to the consolidated IE argument is at the level of structural pattern rather than at the level of lexical cognancy.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the kill-the-grandfather motif as the IE warrior structural-genealogical pattern in the Celtic case; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the typological-rather-than-philological character of the Celtic Lugh-Balor evidential contribution.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Celtic typological-structural contribution)

The Welsh Red-and-White Dragon case is preserved in two principal witnesses with distinct framings — the Latin *Historia Brittonum* (Nennius recension, c. 828 CE) presenting the Britons-vs-Saxons political allegory, and the Middle Welsh *Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion* (manuscripts White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE; Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE) preserving an older non-political reading in which the combat is an annual May-Eve disturbance ended by the king Lludd's mead-pit-and-burial resolution at Dinas Emrys — and the Welsh case is structurally distinctive in the IE-reflex corpus because the combat is between two dragons rather than between a hero and a dragon.footnote:[Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the *Historia Brittonum* and *Lludd and Llefelys* recensions and on the dragon-on-dragon combat structure; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Welsh case's structural distinctiveness within the IE-reflex corpus and on the two-witness side-by-side presentation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Welsh dragon-on-dragon as IE comparative anchor)

The Welsh case is also distinctive in its preservation of an annual-disturbance reading (in *Lludd and Llefelys*) alongside the political-allegorical reading (in *Historia Brittonum*): the May-Eve recurrence of the dragon-combat in *Lludd and Llefelys* shares structural affinity with the Hittite *purulli* annual cycle (treated in C3 / Ch36 §III) and with the Apollo-Python Septerion at Delphi (also a cyclical-cult-foundation reflex), and the Welsh dual recension preserves both the political-prophetic and the cyclical-cosmological dimensions of a single dragon-tradition in two contemporaneous witnesses that the medieval Welsh literary culture transmitted side by side.footnote:[Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the *Lludd and Llefelys* annual-disturbance reading and on the Welsh dual-recension preservation; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the older-substrate reading of *Lludd and Llefelys*; per the C3 (Ch36 §III) framing on the *purulli* and Septerion cyclical-maintenance reflexes for comparative context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material (Welsh dual recension)

The Ossetic Batraz case is preserved in the Nart saga corpus (oral-epic recordings from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators from the 19th and 20th centuries CE), in which the steel-bodied Batraz fights monstrous giants and dragons across multiple episodic combats; Georges Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments — named in body prose without anchoring an EVIDENCE token through them, since Dumézil's own works are not in `sources.yaml` — read Batraz as the Scytho-Alanic IE reflex of the warrior / dragon-slayer pattern, and West 2007 ch. 6 supplies the chapter's IE-comparative anchor for the Ossetic material.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Ossetic Batraz case as the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern (the Scytho-Alanic Indo-European reflex on the Dumézilian reading); per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Ossetic Nart corpus and on Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments as the principal scholarly precedent for the IE-comparative reading; Dumézil cited as named-but-flagged scholar — his specific publications to be added at inventory-audit.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Ossetic Nart corpus, IE reflex)

The Armenian Vahagn case is the most fragmentary of the §III sub-cases: the principal hymn-text to Vahagn is lost, and what survives is only Movses Khorenatsi's late paraphrase at *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31 (conventionally dated 5th–9th c. CE), preserving a triple-birth motif (heavens / sea / reed) and identifying Vahagn as the storm-god, with the dragon-combat content reconstructed from the *Vishapakagh* "dragon-reaper" epithet, from the *vishapakar* stone stelae of the second millennium BCE, and from comparative-philological readings of the Avestan *Vərəθraγna* and the Vedic Indra-Vṛtra material as Indo-Iranian comparanda.footnote:[Russell 1987, *Zoroastrianism in Armenia*, Vahagn chapter, on the lost-hymn-and-late-paraphrase status of the Vahagn material, on the *Vishapakagh* "dragon-reaper" epithet, and on the *vishapakar* stelae; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the explicit fragmentary framing required for the Vahagn material; Petrosyan's Armenian-mythology scholarship cited as named-but-flagged scholar (specific publications to be added at inventory-audit).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Vahagn as Indo-Iranian storm-god / dragon-slayer comparative reflex)

[LACUNA: the principal hymn-text to Vahagn is lost; what survives in primary witness is only Khorenatsi's late paraphrase at *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31, which preserves the triple-birth motif and the *Vishapakagh* epithet but does not preserve a continuous narrative of Vahagn's combat with the *vishaps*. | source: Khorenatsi *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31 (preserving the late paraphrase); the hymn-text itself is not extant in any manuscript witness; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the lost-hymn status | scholarly_reconstruction: Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter reads Khorenatsi's paraphrase as preserving the substantive content of the lost hymn at the level of the dragon-slayer-epithet *Vishapakagh* and the storm-god combat-frame, with the specific verbal-poetic diction of the lost hymn unrecoverable; Petrosyan's Armenian-mythology scholarship corroborates the Russell reading at the level of substantive content while flagging the verbal-philological limits of the reconstruction.]

The Batraz steel-body motif (the Nart hero born from a tumour on his father Khamits's back, plunged into a furnace, and emerging with a body of steel) is read by Dumézilian comparativism as a Scytho-Alanic distinctively-developed feature of the IE warrior-pole, and the death-narrative (Batraz cast from the heavens into a body of water, his steel body cooled-and-shattered against the resistance of the water) has been read by twentieth-century Ossetic-folkloric scholarship as cognate with other IE warrior-death narratives whose specific Old-Norse-and-Ossetic parallels are the subject of continuing comparative-philological discussion.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Batraz steel-body motif and on the death-narrative's IE-comparative reading; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the steel-body and death-by-water motifs; Dumézil cited as named-but-flagged scholar — his specific publications on the Scytho-Alanic warrior-figure to be added at inventory-audit.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Batraz steel-body and death-narrative)

The Slavic case is the chapter's most contested instance: the Ivanov-Toporov 'basic myth' reconstruction proposes that the pre-Christian East Slavic religious tradition contained a structurally central combat-narrative in which the storm-god Perun pursues the cattle-stealing earth-serpent Veles through a four-stage refuge sequence (tree → rock → water → livestock) before striking him with lightning and releasing the rain; the reconstruction draws on three classes of indirect evidence — Slavic folklore items collected in the 19th and 20th centuries, medieval Slavic Christian-period chronicles (*Primary Chronicle*; *Slovo o polku Igoreve*; the 945 CE Russo-Byzantine treaty's oath-formula), and Slavic toponyms.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction including the four-refuge sequence (tree → rock → water → livestock) and the lightning-strike-with-rain-release; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml on the same reconstruction and on the three classes of indirect evidence; per glossary.yaml on "basic myth (Ivanov-Toporov)" first-mention gloss "Ivanov and Toporov's 1965–74 reconstruction of a shared Slavic storm-god-vs-serpent narrative, drawn from folklore and toponyms, and now criticised for methodological circularity"; Ivanov and Toporov 1965–1974 cited as named-but-flagged scholars (their specific Russian-language publications to be added at inventory-audit).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction)

[VARIANT: primary=Ivanov and Toporov's 1965–1974 reconstruction of the Perun-Veles 'basic myth' as a four-stage pursuit ending in a lightning-strike that releases the rain (cited via West 2007 ch. 6, since Ivanov and Toporov's Russian-language monographs are not pinned in `sources.yaml`) | alt=the dissenting position of Roman Jakobson, Patrice Lajoye, Kazimierz Moszyński, and Mate Kapović that the reconstruction depends on circular reasoning between toponym evidence and combat-narrative, on late-and-Christianised chronicle sources, and on over-interpretation of folklore fragments (these scholars named-but-flagged here per the Ch20 framing — their specific publications are not in `sources.yaml` and would be added at inventory-audit) | chosen: present-both | reason: per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml special_instructions and per the locked Ch20 framing, the chapter must present the Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction and the area-specialist dissent fairly side-by-side, since the reconstruction is widely cited but methodologically contested and the chapter's IE-comparative argument cannot rely on it as a confirmed pre-Christian narrative.]

The Slavic case's evidential floor is therefore low and its evidential ceiling is moderate: West 2007 ch. 6 surveys both the reconstruction and the dissenting positions and treats the reconstruction as a structurally-coherent reading of the indirect evidence rather than as a confirmed pre-Christian Slavic combat-narrative, and the chapter accordingly treats the Slavic Perun-Veles case as a §IV "weak" case rather than as a §II direct case.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the standing of the Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction within current Slavic-and-Indo-European-studies scholarship as a structurally-coherent but evidentially-limited reading; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml special_instructions on the reconstruction's contested status and on the chapter's responsibility to present both sides fairly.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (reconstruction's evidential standing)

The East Slavic Dobrynya Nikitich vs. Zmey Gorynych *bylina* — preserved in 19th-century field-recordings from Russian peasant performers in the Russian North (principally Pavel Rybnikov in the 1860s and Aleksandr Hilferding in 1873) — supplies the chapter's Slavic-side attested-witness counterpart to the contested reconstruction: the *bogatyr* Dobrynya kills the three-headed dragon Zmey Gorynych ("Mountain-son") in a three-stage combat (first encounter at the Puchai-river; truce; second combat with princess-abduction and dragon-killing), with the *bylina* tradition reaching back in some form to the medieval Kievan period even though the recorded songs are 19th-century.footnote:[Bailey and Ivanova 1998 (eds.), *An Anthology of Russian Folk Epics*, introduction and Dobrynya-cycle headnotes on the Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych *bylina* and on the three-stage plot-skeleton; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml on the *bylina* tradition's 19th-century-recording / medieval-tradition two-tier dating.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=bailey-ivanova-1998 ; loc=introduction and Dobrynya-cycle headnotes

The Ivanov-Toporov claim that the Dobrynya *bylina* is the later epic reflex of the reconstructed Perun-Veles 'basic myth' (with Dobrynya inheriting the role of Perun and Zmey Gorynych inheriting the role of Veles) is a comparative-structural argument rather than a directly-attested feature of the *bylina* tradition: the recorded *byliny* do not name Perun or Veles, do not invoke the deity-pair, and do not present themselves as preserving older mythological material, and the reflex-claim's evidential weight is therefore doubly mediated through the contested pre-Christian reconstruction and through the structural reading of the Christian-period epic.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on Ivanov and Toporov's reflex-claim as a comparative argument; Bailey and Ivanova 1998, on the *bylina*'s heroic-epic genre and its differences from the reconstructed cosmogonic-and-agricultural Perun-Veles material; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml on the doubly-mediated character of the reflex-claim; Forsyth 1987 ch. 2 on the international Christian-period dragon-slaying type as a competing-or-supplementary explanation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (reflex-claim as comparative argument)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=bailey-ivanova-1998 ; loc=introduction (bylina genre vs. reconstructed myth)

The §III extended-cases dossier therefore covers eight named instances (Greek Zeus-Typhon; Norse Thor-Jörmungandr and Sigurd-Fáfnir; *Beowulf*-dragon; Lugh-Balor; Welsh Red-and-White; Batraz; Vahagn-vishaps; Slavic Perun-Veles and Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych) at evidential strengths ranging from the Norse-skaldic-and-Eddic strong cases (where Watkins's chapter 42 reads the diction as direct IE-formula reflex) to the Slavic and Armenian fragmentary or contested cases (where the reflex-claim is reconstructive or comparative-structural rather than direct philological); §IV develops the strong / weak distinction.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the §III extended-cases gradient; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section III on the eight named instances and the gradient framing; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml and brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml on the philological-evidence-spectrum ordering of the family-chapter sub-cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (extended-cases gradient)

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch11 (Zeus-Typhon) and Ch12 (Greek dragons after Typhon) — the Greek Zeus-Typhon case is treated more fully in Ch11; the present chapter reads it as a Hellenic instance of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern, and the §III treatment defers the Near-Eastern-transmission question to C6 (Ch39).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch18 (Thor-Jörmungandr) — back-link to the Norse-side anchor (Larrington 2014 *Poetic Edda*; Faulkes 1987 *Snorra Edda*; Watkins 1995 Part V on the skaldic diction); the §III treatment of Thor-Jörmungandr is restricted to the formula-reflex argument at the level of the skaldic kennings and does not develop the Ragnarök eschatological reading that C4 develops.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch19 (IE reflexes) — back-link to the family-chapter anchor (Larrington 2014 *Fáfnismál*; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on Sigurd; Tolkien 1936 *Beowulf*; West 2007 ch. 6 on Lugh-Balor and the Welsh dragons; Russell 1987 on Vahagn; West 2007 ch. 6 on Batraz); the present chapter consolidates the family-chapter material into a single IE-formula argument with explicit philological-evidence gradient.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch20 (Slavic-Baltic) — back-link to the Slavic-side anchor (West 2007 ch. 6 on the Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction and on the Baltic Perkūnas / Pērkons name-cognancy; Bailey & Ivanova 1998 on the Dobrynya *bylina*); the §III treatment renders the Slavic case as the chapter's most contested instance with explicit fair presentation of the area-specialist dissent (Jakobson, Lajoye, Moszyński, Kapović).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C6. Transmission (Ch39) — the Greek-Hittite philological connection in the Zeus-Typhon material (West 1997 *East Face of Helicon*; Burkert 1992 *Orientalizing Revolution*) is treated at length in C6 as the Near-Eastern-transmission question; the present chapter notes the connection but does not adjudicate the transmission question, since Watkins's argument is about IE inheritance rather than about contact-borrowing.


=== IV. Where the argument is strong, where it is weak

The §IV strong-and-weak section sorts the eleven IE reflexes treated in §§II–III along the philological-evidence gradient that the chapter has been building. The §II direct cases — Vedic Indra-Vṛtra, Avestan Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka, Hittite Tarḫunna-Illuyanka — sit at the strong end on the strength of regular phonological correspondences across attested-text witnesses; the §III extended cases vary across a wider range, from strong (Norse Sigurd) to weak (Slavic Perun-Veles, Armenian Vahagn).footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the strong-and-weak gradient as a methodological feature of the IE-comparative argument; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section IV on the strong-and-weak framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (strong-and-weak gradient)

The argument is strongest where three conditions are jointly satisfied: (1) attested-text witness in a daughter language with a high-prestige textual record, (2) preserved verb-and-object pairing whose cognates with PIE *gʷhen-* and the noun-slot reflexes are documented by regular sound-law correspondences, and (3) corroborating cognancy at the level of the named figures (Trita Āptya / Thraētaona; *ahi* / *aži*) or at the level of registered poetic diction (Norse skaldic kennings) — the Vedic, Avestan, Hittite, and Norse-Sigurd cases jointly satisfy all three conditions.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the three-condition strength-criterion; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section IV on the Vedic-Avestan-Hittite direct linguistic correspondence and on the Norse skaldic kennings as the strongest cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (three-condition strength-criterion)

The Vedic case is strongest because the attestation is continuous (the Rigveda is orally preserved with extreme precision; the Vṛtra-corpus comprises at least eight hymns; the *áhann áhim* phrasing recurs across the corpus rather than being a one-line feature of a single hymn) and because the formula's verbal slots are filled by inherited words whose Proto-Indo-Iranian etymologies are independently established outside the combat-context, so that the formula's recurrence is not the only evidence for its lexical components.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the continuous Vedic attestation of the Vṛtra-corpus and on the *áhann áhim* recurrence; Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Proto-Indo-Iranian etymologies of *han-* and *ahi-*; Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the Vedic case's strongest-case status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=Vṛtra-corpus continuous attestation
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Vedic strongest-case status)

The Hittite case is the second strongest on the strength of its chronological depth (Hittite as the earliest-attested IE language; the formula's presence in CTH 321 implying a pre-Anatolian / non-Anatolian-divergence stratum) and on the strength of the verb-and-object pairing being preserved in actual primary text (CTH 321) whose philological *editio princeps* is Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the Hittite chronological depth and on the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing in CTH 321; Hoffner 1998 and Beckman 1982, on the Hittite primary text; per brief 06-illuyanka.yaml on the Hittite material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite second-strongest-case status)

The Avestan case is strong on philological grounds (the Vedic-Avestan cognancy at *ahi-* / *aži-* and *han-* / *jan-* is a textbook Indo-Iranian pair) but evidentially limited on narrative grounds (the Avestan corpus preserves only hymn-allusive attestations; the continuous narrative is in the post-Achaemenid Pahlavi *Bundahišn*); the philological signal is therefore stronger than the narrative-attestation signal in the Avestan case, and the chapter notes both.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Vedic-Avestan philological cognancy and on the hymn-allusive character of the Avestan attestations; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae on the absence of a continuous Avestan narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Vedic-Avestan philological cognancy

The Norse Sigurd-Fáfnir case is the strongest of the §III extended cases on Watkins's chapter 42 reading: the Old Norse verb-and-object pairing in *Fáfnismál* is read by Watkins as a direct philological reflex of the IE formula at the level of the verbal diction of the killing-stroke itself, and the Codex Regius preservation supplies a continuous primary-text witness whose philological apparatus is well-developed in twentieth-century Old Norse scholarship.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on Sigurd as the strongest philological evidence in the post-Anatolian, post-Indo-Iranian corpus and on the *Fáfnismál* diction as direct IE-formula reflex; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Sigurd-as-strongest evidence.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42 (Sigurd strongest §III case)

The Norse Thor-Jörmungandr case is strong principally at the level of the registered-poetic-diction kennings — *umbgjǫrð allra landa* "band of all lands" and similar phrases for the Midgard Serpent — that Watkins reads as preserving the IE serpent-combat vocabulary in the registered poetic diction of skaldic verse; the iconographic dossier (Altuna, Hørdum, Gosforth, Ardre VIII runestones) supplies a third register of attestation alongside the Eddic-and-prose textual witnesses.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the Norse skaldic kennings; Larrington 2014 and Faulkes 1987 on the Eddic-and-prose Thor-and-Jörmungandr material; per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the iconographic dossier.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Norse skaldic kennings)

The Old English *Beowulf* case is strong philologically (Watkins reads the dragon-vocabulary and killing-vocabulary as IE-formula reflex) and structurally (Tolkien 1936's load-bearing critical move re-established the mythic register of the monsters), but it is also distinctive in narrative-thematic register: Beowulf's dragon-combat is end-of-life and death-bringing rather than heroic-initiatory, structurally paralleling Thor's Ragnarök mutual-killing rather than Sigurd's youthful ambush.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the *Beowulf* IE-formula reflex; Tolkien 1936 on the mythic-register critical move; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the aged-king-dies-killing-the-dragon thematic distinction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Beowulf formula reflex)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proc. British Academy 22 (1936)

The argument is weaker — but not absent — at the §III cases where the philological signal is replaced or supplemented by typological-structural signal, by fragmentary attestation, or by reconstruction from indirect evidence: the Greek Zeus-Typhon case (where the Hellenic reflex is structurally clear but the philological cognancy with the Indo-Iranian or Anatolian witnesses is contested by the Near-Eastern-transmission reading), the Celtic Lugh-Balor case (one-eyed-giant adversary rather than serpent; sling-stone rather than thunderbolt; the IE reflex reading depends on Dumézilian comparative scholarship rather than on direct philological cognancy), and the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon case (dragon-on-dragon combat structure rather than hero-and-dragon combat structure; the Welsh case stands at the edge of the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT pattern rather than as a central instance).footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Celtic and Welsh cases as morphologically-distinctive IE reflexes; Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the structural distinctiveness of the Welsh dragon-on-dragon combat; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the morphological-distinctiveness preservation in the IE-comparative apparatus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Celtic and Welsh morphological distinctiveness)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material (Welsh dragon-on-dragon)

The Ossetic Batraz case is weaker still on philological grounds (the Nart corpus is 19th- and 20th-century oral-epic rather than continuous primary-text witness; the IE-reflex reading is principally a Dumézilian comparative-scholarship reading rather than a direct philological cognancy) but stronger on structural-functional grounds (Batraz is centrally located in the Ossetic-and-cognate Nart corpora as a warrior-figure of a load-bearing epic tradition); twentieth-century Caucasian-area-specialist scholarship has at times raised methodological reservations about the directness of the Dumézilian IE-comparative reading, and the chapter registers those reservations.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Ossetic Batraz case as the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern on the Dumézilian reading; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Caucasian-area-specialist reservations about the directness of the Dumézilian reading; Dumézil's own publications are named-but-flagged here for inventory-audit follow-up since they are not in `sources.yaml`.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Ossetic Batraz, Dumézilian reading)

The Armenian Vahagn case is among the weakest of the §III extended cases on the strength of the lost-hymn status of the principal source: Khorenatsi's late paraphrase at *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31 preserves a triple-birth motif and the *Vishapakagh* "dragon-reaper" epithet but does not preserve a continuous narrative of Vahagn's combat with the *vishaps*, and the chapter's combat-content is reconstructed from the epithet, the *vishapakar* second-millennium-BCE stelae, and comparative-philological readings against the Avestan *Vərəθraγna* and the Vedic Indra-Vṛtra material; the Vahagn case's evidential basis is fragmentary in a way that none of the other §III sub-cases is.footnote:[Russell 1987, *Zoroastrianism in Armenia*, Vahagn chapter, on the lost-hymn status, on the Khorenatsi paraphrase, and on the four-source reconstruction methodology; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the explicit fragmentary framing required for the Vahagn material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The Slavic Perun-Veles case is the chapter's weakest instance: the Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction is widely cited but methodologically contested by area-specialists Roman Jakobson, Patrice Lajoye, Kazimierz Moszyński, and Mate Kapović, on three principal grounds — circularity in the use of toponym evidence, the late-and-Christianised character of the chronicle attestations, and over-interpretation of folklore fragments whose connection to the reconstructed combat is itself an inference rather than an attested feature of the items.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the three lines of methodological objection (toponym circularity; late-and-Christianised chronicles; folklore over-interpretation); per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml special_instructions on the load-bearing requirement that the chapter present the objections fairly; Jakobson, Lajoye, Moszyński, and Kapović cited as named-but-flagged scholars here per the locked Ch20 framing — their specific publications would be added at inventory-audit.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Slavic methodological-objections survey)

The toponym-circularity objection — that the etymological identification of a place-name as "Perun-derived" or "Veles-derived" presupposes the deity-pair whose existence the toponym is then taken to support — is one of three lines of methodological attack on the Ivanov-Toporov reconstruction, and the chapter registers it without ruling on whether it is decisive against the reconstruction or merely against the strongest reading of it.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the toponym-circularity objection within the methodological critique; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml on the chapter's responsibility to present the objection without ruling on its decisiveness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (toponym-circularity objection)

The late-and-Christianised-chronicles objection — that the *Primary Chronicle*, the *Slovo o polku Igoreve*, and the 945 CE Russo-Byzantine treaty's oath-formula are products of a Christianised literary culture whose representation of pre-Christian deities cannot be taken as direct testimony to pre-Christian belief — is a second line of methodological attack on the reconstruction, and the chapter registers it as one of the principal pressures on the reading of the chronicles as evidence for a pre-Christian combat-narrative.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the late-and-Christianised-chronicles objection; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml special_instructions on the Christianised-literary-culture critique.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (late-and-Christianised-chronicles objection)

The folklore-over-interpretation objection — that the four-stage pursuit-and-refuge folktale type, the harvest-sheaf ritual, and the weather-charm material are folklore items with their own functions and their own histories within a centuries-long Christian-period folk culture, and that reading them as preserving fragments of a pre-Christian combat-narrative imposes a comparative-mythological frame that the items do not themselves require — is the third line of methodological attack on the reconstruction, and the chapter registers it as the most directly load-bearing on the reconstruction's evidential ceiling.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the folklore-over-interpretation objection and on the cross-cultural distribution of the four-refuge folktale type; per brief 20-slavic-baltic.yaml on the international-folktale-type reservation about the four-refuge sequence.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (folklore-over-interpretation objection)

The strong-and-weak gradient is also visible at the level of the figural cognancies that supplement the verbal-formulaic reflexes. The Indo-Iranian Trita / Thraētaona pair, the Indo-Iranian *ahi* / *aži* nominal pair, the Iranian / Armenian *Vərəθraγna* / *Vahagn* pair (with the *Vishapakagh* "dragon-reaper" epithet), and the Greek *typhōs* / *typhōn* root all instantiate, at varying levels of philological tightness, the same structure-of-figural-cognancy that supports the verb-and-object cognancies of §II; the chapter notes the figural-cognancy register without making it a separate sub-argument, since Watkins's primary focus is on the verbal-formulaic register and the figural cognancies are best treated as supplementary evidence within the consolidated philological argument.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Trita / Thraētaona and *ahi* / *aži* Indo-Iranian figural cognancies; Russell 1987, *Zoroastrianism in Armenia*, on the Iranian *Vərəθraγna* / Armenian *Vahagn* pair; Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the verbal-formulaic primary focus and on the figural cognancies as supplementary evidence; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the *Vishapakagh* epithet's load-bearing-philological status for the Vahagn case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita / Thraētaona; ahi / aži cognancies
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter (Vərəθraγna / Vahagn pair)

The chapter's overall reading of the strong-and-weak gradient is therefore that Watkins's argument is well-supported at its philological core (Vedic / Avestan / Hittite, with strong Norse-Sigurd and Old English *Beowulf* extensions) and is increasingly typological-and-reconstructive at its edges (Greek, Celtic, Welsh; Ossetic, Armenian, Slavic), with the Slavic Perun-Veles case sitting at the weakest end of the gradient and not bearing significant evidential weight in the consolidated argument; this is the chapter's principal finding about the IE-comparative argument's evidential profile.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the philological core's strength; West 2007 ch. 6 on the typological-and-reconstructive edges; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section IV on the strong-and-weak framing as the chapter's principal finding about the evidential profile.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (philological-core strength)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (typological-and-reconstructive edges)

[SPECULATION: the chapter's reading of the Watkins formula as primarily an *IE-inheritance* argument — supported at its philological core but increasingly typological at its edges — supports the more measured conclusion that the formula is best read as a Proto-Indo-European *poetic* inheritance rather than as a Proto-Indo-European *theological* or *narrative* inheritance, since the daughter-language reflexes vary in their theology and in their narrative content while sharing the verbal-formulaic diction; this is a methodological speculation about the inheritance's level rather than a claim about any specific reflex | basis: Watkins 1995's volume-title and methodological-framing chapters on the formula as poetic inheritance; the daughter-language reflexes' theological and narrative variation across Vedic ritual cosmology, Avestan eschatology, Hittite festival aetiology, Norse mutual-killing eschatology, and Old English heroic-elegy supports the reading at the level of consonance-with-data | counterargument: a structural-functional comparativist could read the same data as evidence for a Proto-Indo-European *combat-myth* (with both poetic and theological-narrative dimensions inherited together) rather than only for a poetic inheritance, on the grounds that the *ahi* / *aži* nominal cognancy and the Trita / Thraētaona / Vahagn / Vərəθraγna figural cognancies suggest that more than the verbal diction was inherited, and the chapter does not adjudicate between the poetic-only and combat-myth-as-such readings — the chapter leaves the question open while flagging the more measured poetic-inheritance reading as the chapter's preferred working interpretation.]

[INFERENCE: the chapter's classification of the Greek Zeus-Typhon case as one of the weaker §III extended cases — rather than as a §II direct case alongside Vedic, Avestan, and Hittite — depends on the reading that the Greek combat is structurally clear but philologically not as directly cognate as the Indo-Iranian and Anatolian witnesses, and that the Near-Eastern-transmission reading developed in West 1997 and Burkert 1992 introduces a non-IE-inheritance route by which the Greek pattern could have arrived; the inference is consonant with West 1997 and Burkert 1992 but is the chapter's editorial classification rather than a direct claim of either source. | basis: West 1997 *East Face of Helicon* and Burkert 1992 *Orientalizing Revolution* on the Near-Eastern-transmission reading; Watkins 1995 ch. 46 on the Hittite-and-Greek (Typhoeus / Illuyanka) pairing as Watkins handles it; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission question | risk: a Hellenic-philologist defender of the Hesiodic IE-reflex reading could classify Zeus-Typhon as a §II direct case on the strength of *typhōeus* / *typhō*-element analyses; the chapter's classification reflects the C6 transmission-question framing rather than ruling out the IE-reflex reading.]

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch33 (Methods) — back-link to the methods sidebar's discussion of the philological-comparative-method's evidential profile and on the strong / weak gradient as a feature of comparative philology more generally; the present chapter's §IV strong-and-weak framing is the comparative-chapter realisation of the methods sidebar's discipline-on-evidence-gradients.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch20 (Slavic-Baltic) and Ch33 (Methods) — the Slavic Perun-Veles case is treated more fully in Ch20 as the contested-reconstruction case, and its presentation in §IV here registers the methodological objections fairly per the brief's load-bearing requirement; the case feeds Ch33 as a paradigmatic case study in the limits of mythological reconstruction.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch19 (IE reflexes) — the §III extended-cases gradient is consolidated in Ch19's family-chapter format with its six sub-cases (Sigurd, Beowulf, Lugh-Balor, Welsh Red-and-White, Batraz, Vahagn); the present chapter's §IV strong-and-weak ordering is the philological-evidence-spectrum reading that Ch19 prepares.


=== V. Transmission vs. inheritance

The §V section makes explicit the distinction on which the chapter's whole argument rests: Watkins's claim is *inheritance* from a Proto-Indo-European poetic tradition, not *contact-borrowing* from one tradition into another, and this distinction is methodologically distinct from — and complementary to — the transmission question that C6 (Ch39) addresses for the Near-East-to-Greek route in the Kumarbi-and-Typhonomachy material.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters and Parts IV–V, on the inheritance-from-PIE-poetic-tradition reading; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section V on the inheritance-vs-contact-borrowing distinction; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission question that the present chapter does not develop.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (inheritance-vs-contact-borrowing distinction)

Inheritance and transmission are two distinct mechanisms for explaining cross-tradition similarity. Inheritance accounts for the similarity by tracing both daughter-traditions to a shared ancestor whose features are preserved by descent (the IE family-tree model in which Vedic, Avestan, Hittite, Greek, Norse, and other daughter-language reflexes are descendants of a common Proto-Indo-European ancestor); transmission accounts for the similarity by tracing one tradition's features to contact with another, with the receiving tradition acquiring the feature from the donor tradition by cultural-or-textual borrowing (the West 1997 / Burkert 1992 reading of the Hesiodic Typhonomachy as derived from the Kumarbi Cycle by Near-Eastern mediation).footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters, on the inheritance / transmission methodological distinction in IE-comparative work; West 1997, *East Face of Helicon*, on the Near-Eastern-to-Greek transmission reading as the contrasting mechanism; Burkert 1992, *Orientalizing Revolution*, on the same transmission reading; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the West-Burkert transmission-reading for the Greek case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (inheritance / transmission distinction)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=East Face of Helicon (transmission as the contrasting mechanism)

The two mechanisms are not mutually exclusive: a daughter-tradition can inherit a pattern from its IE ancestor and *also* be reinforced or modified by contact with a non-IE tradition (the Hesiodic Typhonomachy is plausibly both an IE inheritance — Greek as an IE daughter language with the formula's traces visible in the Hellenic warrior-and-serpent pattern — and a Near-Eastern-influenced reception, with the Kumarbi-Cycle structural correspondences supplementing rather than replacing the inherited pattern); the chapter registers the compatibility of the two mechanisms while presenting Watkins's IE-inheritance argument as the chapter's central focus.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters, on the compatibility of inheritance and transmission; West 1997, *East Face of Helicon*, on the Near-Eastern reinforcement of the Hesiodic pattern alongside Greek's IE-daughter-language status; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the dual reading of the Greek case; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission-half of the dual reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (inheritance-and-transmission compatibility)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=East Face of Helicon (Near-Eastern reinforcement)

Watkins's argument is methodologically distinct from a transmission-based argument in three respects. First, the evidential basis is regular phonological correspondences across daughter languages (a feature of inheritance) rather than structural-or-narrative correspondences across geographically-and-temporally adjacent traditions (a feature of transmission). Second, the chronological depth of the argument extends back to a reconstructed Proto-Indo-European stage (c. 4500–2500 BCE on standard estimates) rather than to a documented contact horizon. Third, the daughter-language reflexes are required to instantiate the same lexical-syntactic structure rather than the same narrative-or-theological content.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters, on the three-feature distinction between inheritance and transmission methods; per glossary.yaml on "Proto-Indo-European" first-mention gloss "the reconstructed common ancestor of the Indo-European language family, spoken c. 4500–2500 BCE; Watkins's HERO SLAY SERPENT formula is a reconstructed PIE poetic element"; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml on the inheritance-from-PIE-poetic-tradition reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (three-feature distinction)

The Hittite case displays the inheritance-and-contact-area complication most clearly. Hittite is a member of the IE family (the Anatolian branch) so the Tarḫunna-Illuyanka material is an IE inheritance candidate at the level of the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing; Hittite is also a Near-Eastern culture in geographical-and-cultural contact with Hurrian-language traditions (the Kumarbi Cycle is Hurrian-language material translated into Hittite at Hattusa) and with the wider Mesopotamian-Levantine cultural sphere, so the Tarḫunna-Illuyanka material is also a transmission candidate at the level of the festival-aetiology and the storm-god-vs-serpent narrative structure.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the Hittite material's IE-inheritance status at the level of the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing; Hoffner 1998, *Hittite Myths*, on the Anatolian / Hurrian / Mesopotamian-Levantine cultural-contact context of the Hittite corpus; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission-half of the Hittite dual reading; per brief 06-illuyanka.yaml on the Hittite-and-Near-Eastern dual context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite IE-inheritance status)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Hittite Myths (Anatolian / Hurrian / Mesopotamian-Levantine context)

The chapter's resolution of the Hittite dual context is the same as Watkins's: the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing's lexical-syntactic structure is best read as IE inheritance because the verb *kuen-* is a regular reflex of PIE *gʷhen-* and the noun *illuyanka-* is a Hittite common noun rather than a Hurrian or Akkadian loanword; the festival-aetiology of *purulli* and the storm-god-vs-serpent narrative structure may have additional Near-Eastern dimensions that C3 (Ch36) and C6 (Ch39) develop, but the philological core of the formula's Hittite reflex is IE-inherited rather than Near-Eastern-transmitted.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the philological-core IE-inheritance reading of the Hittite reflex; per the C3 (Ch36 §III) framing on the *purulli*-festival cyclical-maintenance dimension and per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the Near-Eastern transmission dimension; Hoffner 1998 on the Hittite primary text.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (philological-core IE-inheritance reading)

The Greek Zeus-Typhon case displays the dual context in the opposite direction: the philological-cognancy evidence is comparatively weaker than in the Indo-Iranian or Anatolian cases (Greek does not preserve a verb-and-object pairing as crisply cognate with the *gʷhen-* / *ahi-* slots as Vedic, Avestan, and Hittite do), while the structural-and-narrative evidence for Near-Eastern transmission (Kumarbi-Cycle parallels; Cilician geographical anchoring; Levantine-coast cultic locations) is comparatively stronger; the C6 chapter (Ch39) develops the transmission-side reading at length, and the present chapter notes the dual context without resolving it.footnote:[Watkins 1995, on Greek's reduced philological cognancy with the Indo-Iranian and Anatolian cores; West 1997, *East Face of Helicon*, on the Near-Eastern transmission reading for the Hesiodic Typhonomachy; Burkert 1992 on the same; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission-side reading; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the Cilician geographical anchoring.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Greek philological cognancy reduced
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=East Face of Helicon (Near-Eastern transmission for Greek)

The inheritance / transmission distinction has implications for the chapter's wider book-internal argument. Where inheritance is the explanation, the IE distribution of the combat-myth is best read as a *family* phenomenon whose explanation is genealogical and whose comparative apparatus is the IE-language-family-tree; where transmission is the explanation, the cross-tradition similarity is a *contact-cultural* phenomenon whose explanation is historical-cultural and whose comparative apparatus is the documentation of borrowing-events.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters, on the family-tree-vs-contact-cultural framing; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section V on the inheritance-vs-transmission distinction's bearing on the chapter's wider argument; per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the book-internal typological-and-comparative framework.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (family-tree vs. contact-cultural)

The chapter's overall position on the inheritance / transmission distinction is therefore that they are complementary rather than competing methods, that Watkins's IE-inheritance argument is the chapter's central focus and is best supported at the §II direct cases (Vedic / Avestan / Hittite philological core), that transmission is the C6 chapter's territory and is best supported at the Greek-Hittite-Levantine intersection (where Hesiod and the Kumarbi Cycle and the Baal-Yamm material plausibly meet), and that the two methods together display the variety of mechanisms by which combat-myth material crosses tradition-boundaries.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters, on the complementarity of inheritance and transmission; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml on the chapter's IE-inheritance focus; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission-half of the methodological pair; West 1997 *East Face of Helicon* on the Greek-Hittite-Levantine transmission intersection.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (inheritance-and-transmission complementarity)

A further methodological feature of the inheritance / transmission distinction is its bearing on what counts as a successful demonstration. An inheritance argument is successful when regular phonological correspondences across daughter languages support the reconstruction of a Proto-Indo-European feature whose preserved descendants are the daughter-language reflexes; a transmission argument is successful when documented contact between traditions, attested borrowing-events, or shared specific-narrative-detail patterns support the diffusion of a feature from a donor-tradition to a receiving-tradition; the two argument-types have different evidential standards and the chapter is explicit that Watkins's argument is to be evaluated on the inheritance standard rather than on the transmission standard.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters, on the different evidential standards for inheritance and transmission arguments; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section V on the chapter's responsibility to evaluate Watkins's argument on the inheritance standard; per the C6 (Ch39) framing on the transmission-standard evaluation that the C6 chapter performs for the Near-East-to-Greek case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (different evidential standards)
