The chapter's IE-inheritance argument is therefore a *bounded* finding: it explains the IE distribution of the combat-myth at the level of philological inheritance, and it does not — and is not designed to — explain the existence of the combat-myth pattern in non-IE traditions; the §VI Mwindo control develops this boundedness explicitly, since the existence of non-IE serpent-combat traditions with their own combat-vocabularies shows that the IE-inheritance argument is one finding among the family of comparative-mythological findings that the book's typology requires.footnote:[Watkins 1995, on the IE-inheritance argument as a finding about the IE distribution rather than about the global existence of combat-myth; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the bounded character of Watkins's argument and on the §VI Mwindo control as the chapter's closing typological corrective.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (bounded IE-inheritance finding)

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C6. Transmission (Ch39) — the transmission question (Near East → Greece via Hurrian-Hittite-Levantine intermediaries; Kumarbi-Cycle and Baal-Yamm reception in Hesiod) is C6's territory; the present chapter's §V framing draws the methodological contrast between IE-inheritance and contact-transmission, and the C6 chapter develops the contact-transmission reading at length on the Hesiodic-Typhonomachy and Greek-Levantine material.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch5 (Kumarbi Cycle) and Ch11 (Zeus-Typhon) — the Hurrian-language Kumarbi Cycle and the Greek Zeus-Typhon material together form the principal exhibit for the C6 transmission argument; the present chapter notes the Greek case's dual context (IE-inheritance and Near-Eastern transmission) without adjudicating between them, and the C6 chapter takes up the transmission-side reading.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch33 (Methods) — back-link to the methods sidebar's discussion of inheritance-and-transmission as distinct comparative methods; the present chapter's §V framing of the methodological distinction is the comparative-chapter realisation of the methods sidebar's framework.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" — the inheritance-vs-transmission distinction is methodologically distinct from the in-tradition vs. cross-tradition reconstruction-policy distinction that scope.md operationalises; both distinctions are comparative-method discriminations, but the inheritance-vs-transmission pair concerns the *mechanism* of cross-tradition similarity while the reconstruction-policy pair concerns the *legitimacy* of cross-tradition narrative-content fills.


=== VI. The Mwindo problem as a control

The §VI Mwindo control is the chapter's closing typological corrective to a possible over-extension of Watkins's argument: if the IE-inheritance reading were sufficient to explain the existence of combat-myth as such (rather than only its IE distribution), then non-IE serpent-combat traditions would either be IE-borrowings or would not exhibit serpent-combat vocabulary; the Mwindo material — together with other non-IE serpent-combat traditions noted in the book's wider catalogue — shows that this implication does not hold, and the chapter therefore registers the Mwindo case as the methodological control that limits Watkins's argument to its proper IE scope.footnote:[Biebuyck and Mateene 1969, *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*, on the Nyanga Mwindo cycle and its serpent-combat material; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section VI on the Mwindo problem as a control; per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the in-scope / contrastive-tier status of the Mwindo case in the book's catalogue.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (control case)

The Mwindo Epic is a Nyanga oral-epic tradition recorded from the Banyanga of the eastern Democratic Republic of the Congo by Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene in the 1950s and published in critical edition with English translation in 1969 (University of California Press); the recorded version (the Karisi-version of Shé-Kárisi Rureke) presents the hero Mwindo's birth, exile, descent into the underworld, return, and his contests against various adversaries — including the dragon Kirimu — across an extended epic-narrative.footnote:[Biebuyck and Mateene 1969, *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*, introduction and translation, on the Karisi-version of Shé-Kárisi Rureke and on Mwindo's epic-narrative structure; per glossary.yaml on "Mwindo" first-mention gloss "Nyanga epic hero; kills the dragon Kirimu and is punished by the sky-god Nkuba — the contrastive case".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=introduction and translation

Mwindo's dragon-killing of Kirimu is one of the cycle's named adversary-killings, and it occurs alongside other Mwindo combats that include adversaries who are not serpent-or-dragon morphologies; the dragon-combat material in the Mwindo cycle is not a structural-typological focal point of the cycle in the way the Vedic Vṛtra-combat is the structural-typological focal point of the Indra-cycle in the Rigveda, and the Mwindo cycle's narrative interest is broader than dragon-combat.footnote:[Biebuyck and Mateene 1969, on Mwindo's combat with Kirimu and on the wider adversary-cycle of the Mwindo epic; per glossary.yaml on Kirimu first-mention gloss "Nyanga dragon slain by Mwindo".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo cycle (Kirimu combat in wider epic-narrative)

The Mwindo case's distinctive feature for the chapter's typological argument is that Mwindo's killing of Kirimu is followed by the hero's *punishment* by the sky-god Nkuba — the killing is treated within the Nyanga cosmology not as a heroic-deed-warranting-celebration but as a transgression that requires expiation, and the cycle's resolution is not the hero's elevation to sovereignty (as in the Indra-Vṛtra and Zeus-Typhon cases) but the hero's chastening and reintegration into a moral-cosmic order whose terms the dragon-combat had threatened.footnote:[Biebuyck and Mateene 1969, on Mwindo's punishment by Nkuba after the killing of Kirimu and on the moral-cosmic-order resolution of the cycle; per glossary.yaml on Nkuba first-mention gloss "Nyanga sky-god; punishes Mwindo for the killing of Kirimu" and on Mwindo as "the contrastive case"; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the Mwindo control's contrastive function.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo cycle (punishment by Nkuba)

The Mwindo case shows that the global pattern of "hero-kills-serpent" is not exclusively an IE-inheritance pattern: a fully-formed dragon-killing narrative is preserved in a non-IE oral-epic tradition (Nyanga is a Bantu language family member, not an IE language) with its own combat-vocabulary, its own moral-cosmic frame, and its own resolution-pattern; the Mwindo control thus establishes that Watkins's IE-inheritance argument is a finding *about the IE distribution* of the combat-myth, not a finding *about the existence* of dragon-combat narrative as such.footnote:[Biebuyck and Mateene 1969, on the Mwindo cycle as a fully-formed non-IE dragon-killing narrative with its own combat-vocabulary; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the bounded character of Watkins's argument; Watkins 1995 on the IE scope of the argument.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo cycle as non-IE control case
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (IE scope of the argument)

The Mwindo control is reinforced by the existence of other non-IE serpent-combat traditions in the book's wider catalogue — Mesopotamian (Marduk-Tiamat in *Enūma Eliš*, treated in Ch3 and C1), Egyptian (Ra-Apep, treated in Ch16 and C3), Levantine (Baal-Yamm in the Ugaritic Baal Cycle, treated in Ch7 and C2), Mesoamerican (Coyolxauhqui's dismemberment, treated in Ch24), and Andean (the *amaru* serpent-combats, treated in Ch25) — all of which preserve serpent-combat material in languages and cultural contexts outside the IE family; the existence of this wider non-IE attestation is the principal book-internal warrant for the chapter's bounded reading of Watkins's argument.footnote:[Per scope.md §"In-scope catalog" on the non-IE serpent-combat traditions in the book's wider catalogue; per Ch3 (Marduk-Tiamat), Ch16 (Ra-Apep), Ch7 (Baal-Yamm), Ch24 (Coyolxauhqui), Ch25 (Andean *amaru*) on the per-tradition treatments; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml on the wider catalogue's bearing on the Mwindo control argument.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo Epic (in-scope non-IE control case)

The reading of the Mwindo case as a *control* is methodologically distinct from a reading of it as a *parallel*: a control case bounds the scope of an argument by showing what the argument does not explain (in this case, the IE-inheritance argument does not explain the Nyanga dragon-killing, which falls outside the IE family); a parallel case extends an argument by showing further instances within its scope (which the Mwindo case is not, since Nyanga is not an IE language); the chapter uses the Mwindo material as control rather than as parallel and is explicit about the distinction.footnote:[Per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the Mwindo case as control rather than as parallel; per scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" and §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the methodological discipline of distinguishing controls from parallels in cross-tradition comparison.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo Epic (control vs. parallel distinction)

[INFERENCE: the Mwindo case's classification as a *control* on Watkins's argument depends on the prior assumption that the dragon-killing in the Mwindo cycle is genuinely typologically a dragon-killing (and not, e.g., a metaphorical-narrative element that does not bear the same structural weight as the IE dragon-combats); the chapter's reading rests on Biebuyck and Mateene's treatment of the Kirimu-killing as a substantive narrative episode in the cycle and on the brief's framing of the Mwindo material as the contrastive case for the chapter's typological argument. | basis: Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 on the Mwindo cycle's Kirimu-combat as a substantive narrative episode; brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section VI on the Mwindo control framing | risk: a Bantuist area-specialist could read the Kirimu-combat as functionally distinct from the IE dragon-combats (e.g., as more akin to a transgression-and-expiation cycle than to a sovereignty-establishing or cosmogonic combat), and the chapter's classification of Mwindo as a control rather than as a parallel reflects the brief's typological framing rather than a Bantuist-area-specialist verdict; the chapter's reading is the comparative-chapter framing rather than a Nyanga-area-specialist claim.]

The §VI Mwindo control is therefore the chapter's closing methodological discipline: Watkins's argument is the strongest linguistic argument for a shared *Indo-European* combat-tradition, and the present chapter has presented that argument with explicit gradient between direct philological evidence (§II) and typological-or-fragmentary evidence (§III), with the Slavic Perun-Veles reconstruction held up to the area-specialist dissent (§IV) and with the inheritance-vs-transmission distinction made explicit (§V); the chapter closes on the Mwindo control to register that this finding is bounded — about the IE distribution of the combat-myth, not about the combat-myth as a global pattern — and to leave open the wider questions about non-IE serpent-combat traditions that the book's other Part-II chapters (C7 inversion problem; C8 contrastive cases) develop in their respective registers.footnote:[Per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's closing-methodological-discipline framing; Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the IE-inheritance argument's scope; Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 on the Nyanga Mwindo material as the chapter's control case; per the C7 (inversion problem) and C8 (contrastive cases) framing on the wider questions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo Epic (closing-methodological-discipline)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (IE-inheritance argument's scope)

The chapter's settled position is therefore that Watkins's HERO SLAY SERPENT formula is a real-and-load-bearing finding about the IE distribution of the combat-myth, that its philological core (Vedic / Avestan / Hittite, with strong Norse-Sigurd and Old English *Beowulf* extensions) is well-supported by regular phonological correspondences across attested-text witnesses, that its evidential edges (Greek; Celtic; Welsh; Ossetic; Armenian; Slavic) are increasingly typological-and-reconstructive and require corresponding methodological care, that the inheritance-vs-transmission distinction situates the formula's argument within a wider methodological landscape whose other dimensions C6 (Ch39) and C7 (Ch40) develop, and that the Mwindo control bounds the argument to its proper IE scope.footnote:[Per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml comparative_argument and special_instructions on the chapter's settled position; Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the formula's philological core and evidential edges; West 2007 ch. 6 on the wider IE storm-god / water-serpent complex; Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 on the Mwindo control.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (settled position on Watkins's formula)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (wider IE storm-god / water-serpent complex)

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch29 (Bida) and C7. Inversion problem (Ch40) — the Bida case (West African Soninke material; the dragon-as-source-of-prosperity whose killing brings drought and exile) is the book's principal non-IE inversion-problem exhibit; the present chapter's §VI Mwindo control complements the C7 inversion-problem material by registering a non-IE case in which the dragon-killing has its own moral-cosmic resolution (Mwindo's punishment by Nkuba) that does not fit the IE warrior-and-serpent pattern.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" — the Mwindo control's role as a methodological bounding of Watkins's argument complements the appropriation-protocol discipline that scope.md operationalises for non-Eurasian material; the §VI presentation of the Nyanga case as control rather than as parallel is consonant with the appropriation-protocol's requirement that comparative framings of non-Eurasian material be bounded rather than totalising.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamian (Ch3 Marduk-Tiamat); Egyptian (Ch16 Ra-Apep); Levantine (Ch7 Baal-Yamm); Mesoamerican (Ch24 Coyolxauhqui); Andean (Ch25 *amaru*) — the wider non-IE serpent-combat catalogue reinforces the Mwindo control's methodological function: the global pattern of hero-or-deity-defeats-serpent extends well beyond the IE family, and the IE-inheritance argument explains the IE distribution of the pattern but does not pretend to explain its broader global occurrence.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Contrastive cases (Ch41) — the C8 chapter develops further non-IE contrastive cases (Mapuche, Anishinaabe, Australian Aboriginal Rainbow Serpent material, Andean *amaru*) at length; the present chapter's §VI Mwindo control is the comparative-chapter pre-figuration of C8's broader non-IE survey.

[[ch-38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula]]
== C5. The Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT Formula

=== Source overview

This chapter is a comparative-philological synthesis chapter rather than a primary-source narrative chapter; it draws on two layers of evidence — (1) the IE-comparativist anchor that supplies the chapter's central reconstruction and the bulk of its philological exhibits (Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon*, Parts IV–V), and (2) the per-tradition area-specialist anchors that ground each daughter-language reflex in its own primary-text witness (Jamison & Brereton 2014 for Vedic; Benveniste & Renou 1934 for Avestan; Hoffner 1998 and Beckman 1982 for Hittite; Larrington 2014 and Faulkes 1987 for Old Norse; West 1966 and Ogden 2013 for Greek; Russell 1987 for Armenian; Bailey & Ivanova 1998 for Slavic *bylina*; Tolkien 1936 for the load-bearing critical move on the *Beowulf* dragon).footnote:[Per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml sources.anchor on the four methodological-comparative anchors (Watkins 1995; West 2007; Benveniste & Renou 1934; Jamison & Brereton 2014) and chapters_drawn_from on the eight Part-I chapters (Chs 6, 11, 13, 14, 15, 18, 19, 20) whose own area-specialist anchors carry the per-tradition narrative-claims layer; Watkins, C., *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1995), entire volume — especially Parts IV–V — as the chapter's principal philological anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (HERO SLAY SERPENT formula, the chapter's principal anchor)

The chapter is the fifth of the eight Part-II comparative chapters (C1 through C8) and is positioned as the load-bearing philological chapter for the Indo-European distribution of the combat-myth, distinguished within the Part-II sequence from the cosmogonic (C1), sovereignty (C2), cyclical-maintenance (C3), and eschatological (C4) subtype-chapters by treating the combat across IE daughter languages as the *linguistic*-and-*poetic* fact whose explanation is genealogical inheritance from a shared ancestor rather than as a typological category whose explanation is structural-functional or independent invention.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II on the Part-II comparative chapters' division of labour and on C5's status as the book's IE-philological chapter; per glossary.yaml on "HERO SLAY SERPENT (formula)" first-mention gloss "Calvert Watkins's reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula (subject + verb-of-killing + serpent-object), attested in Vedic áhann áhim, Hittite kuenta... illuyanka, and elsewhere".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (formula as IE poetic inheritance)

The chapter's central argument follows Watkins's 1995 reconstruction: the verbal formula HERO SLAY SERPENT (a subject-noun naming the warrior, a verb of killing, and a serpent-object naming the adversary) is preserved across the IE daughter languages at the level of *lexical-and-syntactic* correspondences (Vedic *áhann áhim*; Avestan cognates; Hittite *kuenta… illuyanka*) rather than at the level of structural-typological resemblance alone, and the philological correspondences are best explained as inheritance from a Proto-Indo-European poetic tradition rather than as contact-borrowing or independent convergence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon*, Parts IV–V on the lexical-and-syntactic basis of the reconstruction and on the Indo-European inheritance reading; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml comparative_argument on the chapter's framing of the reconstruction as the strongest linguistic argument for a shared IE combat-tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (lexical-syntactic inheritance reading)

The chapter holds Watkins's argument open to two distinct kinds of pressure. First, the chapter is explicit about the gradient between direct philological evidence (the Vedic / Avestan / Hittite triple of §II, where Watkins's formula stands on regular lexical correspondences) and typological-or-fragmentary evidence (the §III extended cases, where the philological signal weakens and the typological signal carries more of the comparative weight). Second, the chapter closes with a Mwindo control (§VI) that registers the existence of non-IE serpent-combat traditions with their own combat-vocabularies, showing that IE inheritance is a real finding about the IE distribution of the combat-myth and not a totalising explanation of the pattern's broader global occurrence.footnote:[Per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the respectful-but-bounded presentation of Watkins's argument and on the Mwindo control as the chapter's closing typological corrective; Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 on the Mwindo material as the in-scope non-IE control case for the chapter's typological claim.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (chapter's framing anchor)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (control case)

The Vedic-side anchor is Jamison and Brereton 2014 (the Oxford three-volume Rigveda translation), which supplies the chapter's working English text for the Indra-Vṛtra hymn-corpus and for the Trita Āptya residual stratum that the comparative argument draws on; the philological anchor for the same Indo-Iranian material is Benveniste and Renou 1934, which supplies the foundational philological treatment of the Vedic Vṛtra and Avestan Vərəθraγna together as cognate reflexes of a shared Indo-Iranian "obstruction-and-victory" complex.footnote:[Jamison, S.W. and Brereton, J.P. (trans.), *The Rigveda: The Earliest Religious Poetry of India*, 3 vols. (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014), used as the chapter's working English Rigveda text and as the area-specialist anchor for Indra-Vṛtra (Ch14) and Trita Āptya (Ch15) material; Benveniste, É. and Renou, L., *Vṛtra et Vṛθragna: Étude de mythologie indo-iranienne* (Paris: Imprimerie Nationale, 1934), as the foundational Indo-Iranian philological treatment underlying both the Vedic and Avestan sub-cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=Rigveda 1.32 and supplementary Vṛtra hymns
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Vṛtra et Vṛθragna (Indo-Iranian comparative core)

The Avestan-side anchor is Benveniste and Renou 1934 again, which supplies the chapter's working philological apparatus for the Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka material in the Avestan yashts (Yt. 5.33–35; Yt. 14; Yt. 19) and the Pahlavi *Bundahišn*, and which establishes Trita Āptya / Thraētaona as a cognate Indo-Iranian pair on the basis of regular phonological correspondences between Vedic Sanskrit and Avestan; Watkins 1995 Part V (chs. 44–48) supplies the chapter's IE-comparativist anchor for the Avestan material at the level of the consolidated formula reconstruction.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, *Vṛtra et Vṛθragna*, on the Avestan yasht material (Yt. 5.33–35; Yt. 14; Yt. 19) and on the Trita Āptya / Thraētaona Indo-Iranian cognancy; Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon*, Part V chs. 44–48, on the Avestan reflexes within the consolidated IE formula reconstruction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Avestan yashts and Pahlavi Bundahišn material
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V chs. 44-48 (Avestan reflexes)

The Hittite-side anchor is Hoffner 1998 (the working English translation of CTH 321, second edition, *Hittite Myths*) and Beckman 1982 (the philological *editio princeps* in *JANES* 14: 11–25); Watkins 1995 chapter 46 ("Typhoeus and the Illuyankas") supplies the IE-comparativist treatment of the Hittite verb-and-object pairing *kuenta… illuyanka-* as cognate with Vedic *áhann áhim*.footnote:[Hoffner, H.A., *Hittite Myths*, 2nd ed., SBL Writings from the Ancient World 2 (Atlanta: Society of Biblical Literature, 1998), "Illuyanka" §§1–2; Beckman, G., "The Anatolian Myth of Illuyanka", *JANES* 14 (1982): 11–25, the *editio princeps*; Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing as cognate with Vedic *áhann áhim*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite cognate formulation)

The §III area-specialist anchors are Larrington 2014 and Faulkes 1987 for the Norse witnesses (Thor-Jörmungandr and Sigurd-Fáfnir), Tolkien 1936 for the load-bearing critical move on the *Beowulf* dragon and Watkins 1995 Part V for the *Beowulf* formula reading, West 2007 ch. 6 for the Celtic Lugh-Balor and Welsh Red-and-White Dragon material via Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic, West 2007 ch. 6 for the Ossetic Batraz / Nart corpus, Russell 1987 for the Armenian Vahagn and *vishaps*, West 2007 ch. 6 for the Slavic Perun-Veles reconstruction, and Bailey & Ivanova 1998 for the Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych *bylina*; West 1966 (the Oxford critical edition of Hesiod's *Theogony*) and Ogden 2013 for the Greek Zeus-Typhon material complete the §III dossier.footnote:[Larrington, C. (trans.), *The Poetic Edda*, rev. ed. (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014); Faulkes, A. (trans.), *Edda* by Snorri Sturluson (London: Dent / Everyman, 1987); Tolkien, J.R.R., "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics", *Proc. British Academy* 22 (1936): 245–295; West, M.L., *Indo-European Poetry and Myth* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2007), ch. 6; Ogden, D., *Drakōn* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2013); Russell, J.R., *Zoroastrianism in Armenia*, Harvard Iranian Series 5 (1987); Bailey, J. and Ivanova, T. (eds.), *An Anthology of Russian Folk Epics* (Armonk, NY: M.E. Sharpe, 1998); West, M.L. (ed.), *Hesiod: Theogony* (Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1966).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Codex Regius Sigurd-cycle and Hymiskviða
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 34, 46-48, 51
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proc. British Academy 22 (1936)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Storm and Stream)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=chs. 2-3 and ch. on Celtic material
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter
// EVIDENCE: source_id=bailey-ivanova-1998 ; loc=introduction and Dobrynya-cycle headnotes
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1966-theogony ; loc=Theog. 820-880

The chapter's evidentiary discipline follows the comparative-chapter pattern established at Ch33–Ch36: every per-tradition philological claim is routed through the same `sources.yaml` ID that the corresponding Part-I chapter uses for that fact, rather than through the Part-I brief or chapter draft itself; the §II direct philological cases (Vedic, Avestan, Hittite) typically carry single-token paragraphs anchored on the area specialist or on Watkins, while the §III extended typological cases benefit from doubled tokens (area-specialist + Watkins-or-West comparative anchor) per the Ch34/Ch35 precedent.footnote:[Per the chapter-claims skill instructions on the no-invented-source-IDs rule and on the comparative-chapter skill's in-scope-and-out-of-scope citation requirement; per the precedent set by chapters/34-C1-combat-as-cosmogony.claims.adoc and chapters/35-C2-combat-as-sovereignty.claims.adoc on the doubled-token pattern as needed and on the single-token default elsewhere; Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon*, Parts IV–V, as the chapter's central anchor whose source_id underwrites a large fraction of the EVIDENCE tokens below.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (chapter's central anchor, methodological warrant)

The chapter follows the brief's special_instructions in not re-narrating the eight Part-I cases — the reader has Chs 6 (Illuyanka), 11 (Zeus-Typhon), 13 (Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka), 14 (Indra-Vṛtra), 15 (Vedic reflexes including Trita Āptya), 18 (Thor-Jörmungandr), 19 (IE reflexes — Sigurd, Beowulf, Lugh-Balor, Welsh Red-and-White, Batraz, Vahagn), and 20 (Slavic-Baltic — Perun-Veles, Dobrynya) for the narrative-and-source material — and the comparative chapter's job is to make the philological argument across them rather than to recapitulate their narrative content; the brief's target length of 7,500 body-prose words is on the longer side for Part II, and the chapter's analytical through-line is Watkins's lexical-syntactic reconstruction with explicit gradients between direct, typological, and fragmentary evidence.footnote:[Per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml special_instructions on the respectful-but-bounded presentation of Watkins's argument and on the gradient-of-evidence framing the chapter must register; per chapters/14-indra-vrtra.adoc, chapters/13-thraetaona-azidahaka.adoc, chapters/06-illuyanka.adoc, chapters/18-thor-jormungandr.adoc, chapters/11-zeus-typhon.adoc, chapters/19-ie-reflexes.adoc, chapters/15-vedic-reflexes.adoc, and chapters/20-slavic-baltic.adoc on the per-tradition narrative-and-source treatment that the present chapter does not duplicate.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (chapter's analytical through-line)

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the cosmogonic-subtype chapter treats the cosmos-emerging-from-the-adversary's-body structure (Marduk-Tiamat) as a structural-typological category whose explanation is functional rather than genealogical; the present chapter complements C1 by treating the IE distribution of the combat-myth as a *philological* fact whose explanation is genealogical, and the two chapters together display the contrast between structural-typological and genealogical-philological comparativisms that the book's typology requires.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C2. Combat as sovereignty (Ch35) — the sovereignty-subtype chapter reads Indra-Vṛtra, Baal-Yamm, and Zeus-Typhon as instances of a sovereignty-establishing combat type whose explanation crosses the IE / Near-Eastern boundary; the present chapter treats the IE half of that same material as a philological inheritance internal to the IE family, and §V of the present chapter develops the inheritance-vs-transmission distinction that distinguishes the C5 question from the C2 and C6 questions.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C3. Cyclical maintenance (Ch36) — the Hittite Tarḫunna-Illuyanka case is co-treated in C3 (cyclical-maintenance) and in the present chapter (IE philological inheritance) on the two distinct comparative axes that Watkins's chapter 46 and the *purulli*-festival material respectively support; the same Illuyanka tablets carry both the on-tablet *purulli* embedding (C3) and the *kuenta… illuyanka-* lexical pairing (C5), and the two readings are independent but jointly preserved in the same primary witness.

What follows is organised in six body sections. Section I lays out the formula itself — Watkins's reconstructed HERO SLAY SERPENT structure of subject + verb-of-killing + serpent-object — and the cognate verb-and-object pairings that anchor it in Vedic, Avestan, and Hittite; section II develops the three direct philological cases (Vedic Indra-Vṛtra, Avestan Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka, Hittite Tarḫunna-Illuyanka) at which Watkins's reconstruction stands on regular lexical-and-syntactic correspondences across attested-text witnesses; section III consolidates the eight extended typological cases (Greek Zeus-Typhon; Norse Thor-Jörmungandr and Sigurd-Fáfnir; Old English _Beowulf_-dragon; Celtic Lugh-Balor; Welsh Red-and-White Dragon; Ossetic Batraz; Armenian Vahagn-_vishaps_; Slavic Perun-Veles and Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych) across a philological-evidence spectrum from strongest (Norse Sigurd) to weakest (Slavic Perun-Veles, Armenian Vahagn); section IV sorts the eleven reflexes along the strong-and-weak gradient and presents the area-specialist dissent on the Slavic case; section V makes explicit the inheritance-versus-transmission distinction on which the chapter's whole argument rests and locates the contact-borrowing question in C6 (Ch39); and section VI closes with the Mwindo control, which bounds Watkins's IE-inheritance argument to its proper IE scope without totalising the global combat-myth pattern.

=== I. The formula

The first body section reconstructs the formula itself — the verbal-syntactic structure that the rest of the chapter's argument is built on — and traces its three principal lexical anchors across the IE family.

Calvert Watkins, in *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics* (Oxford University Press, 1995), reconstructs an Indo-European poetic formula of the form HERO SLAY SERPENT, in which a subject-noun naming the warrior, a verb of killing, and a serpent-object naming the adversary together name the combat in a fixed verbal-syntactic structure that the volume's framing chapters lay out as the central philological exhibit of the work.footnote:[Watkins, C., *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics* (New York: Oxford University Press, 1995), Parts IV–V framing chapters on the HERO SLAY SERPENT formula's reconstruction as subject + verb-of-killing + serpent-object, and on the volume's central exhibit-status of the formula; per glossary.yaml on "HERO SLAY SERPENT (formula)" first-mention gloss.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V framing chapters

The formula is not a fixed verbal sequence at the level of word-order — Watkins is explicit that the daughter-language reflexes vary in word-order according to the metrical and syntactic norms of each daughter language — but a fixed *lexical-and-semantic* structure in which three slots (warrior-subject, killing-verb, serpent-object) are filled by inherited words whose etymologies recur across the IE family.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the formula as a lexical-and-semantic structure rather than a fixed surface-word-order sequence, and on the daughter-language word-order variation; per the brief's structure section I on the formula as subject + verb-of-killing + serpent-object.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (lexical-semantic structure, not surface order)

The Vedic reflex anchoring Watkins's reconstruction is *áhann áhim* — "he slew the serpent" — from Rigveda 1.32.2, the second verse of the principal Indra-Vṛtra hymn whose opening verse announces the theme of Indra's manly deeds: the third-person preterite of the verb *han-* ("to strike, to kill") combined with the accusative-singular of *ahi-* ("serpent") supplies a verb-and-object pairing whose etymological components recur across the IE family in cognate forms.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, RV 1.32.1–2, on the opening verse's announcement of Indra's manly deeds and on the *áhann áhim* phrasing in the second verse; Watkins 1995, ch. 46 (and the broader Part V), on *áhann áhim* as the Vedic reflex closest to the reconstructed Indo-European form; per glossary.yaml on the locked rendering "áhann áhim" with diacritics.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 1.32.1-2
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (áhann áhim as reflex anchor)

The Vedic verbal root *han-* ("to strike, to kill") is the principal verb-of-killing in the formula and is etymologically connected, on Watkins's analysis and on the wider Indo-Iranian-comparative literature, with the Avestan verbal root *jan-* and with the Hittite verbal root *kuen-* / *kwen-* (third-singular preterite *kuenta*), all from a reconstructed Proto-Indo-European root *gʷhen-* "to strike".footnote:[Watkins 1995, chs. 41–48, on the *gʷhen-* root and its Vedic *han-* / Avestan *jan-* / Hittite *kuen-* daughter-language reflexes as the verbal-root anchor of the formula's killing-verb slot; Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Indo-Iranian *han-* / *jan-* cognancy.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=chs. 41-48 (PIE *gʷhen- and its daughter-language reflexes)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Indo-Iranian *han- / *jan- cognancy

The Vedic noun *ahi-* ("serpent") is the principal serpent-object of the formula and is philologically cognate, by regular Indo-Iranian sound-laws, with the Avestan noun *aži-*, the same word for "serpent" appearing as the first element in the name of the Avestan dragon-tyrant Aži Dahāka; the Vedic-Avestan *ahi-* / *aži-* pair is one of the cleanest Indo-Iranian noun-cognancies in the dragon-combat lexicon.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Vedic *ahi-* / Avestan *aži-* cognancy as one of the cleanest Indo-Iranian noun-cognancies; Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the same; per glossary.yaml on "ahi" first-mention gloss "'serpent' (Sanskrit); the word for Vṛtra in the áhann áhim formula; philologically cognate with Avestan aži".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Vedic ahi / Avestan aži cognancy
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (ahi / aži cognancy)

The Avestan reflex of the formula is most commonly cited in the form *janat aiim* ("he slew the serpent") and similar verb-noun pairings in the Avestan yashts, in which the Avestan verbal root *jan-* (cognate with Vedic *han-*) and the Avestan noun *aži-* (cognate with Vedic *ahi-*) instantiate the same lexical-and-semantic structure that Vedic *áhann áhim* instantiates in Vedic Sanskrit.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Avestan verb-and-noun pairing in the yashts and on the formula's instantiation in Avestan; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–48, on the Avestan reflex of the formula and on the *jan-* / *han-* and *aži-* / *ahi-* cognancy basis; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml comparative_argument naming "*janat aiim*" as the Avestan reflex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Avestan yashts (verb-noun pairing)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V chs. 44-48 (Avestan reflex)

The Hittite reflex of the formula is *kuenta… illuyanka-*, "he slew… the serpent", from CTH 321 (the Illuyanka tablets), in which the Hittite verbal root *kuen-* (third-singular preterite *kuenta*) is cognate with Vedic *han-* and Avestan *jan-* under the regular Anatolian / non-Anatolian sound-law correspondence, and the noun *illuyanka-* is the common Hittite word for "serpent" used as the proper name of the storm-god's adversary in the same tablets.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the Hittite verb-and-object pairing *kuenta… illuyanka-* in CTH 321 and on the Anatolian / non-Anatolian sound-law correspondence underlying the verbal-root cognancy with Vedic *han-* and Avestan *jan-*; Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2, on the Hittite text containing the pairing; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25, on the *editio princeps*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite cognate formulation)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2

The cross-language identity of the formula is therefore *philological* in a strict sense: the verb-slot is filled by a reflex of PIE *gʷhen-* in each of the three daughter languages (Vedic *han-* / Avestan *jan-* / Hittite *kuen-*), and the noun-slot is filled by reflexes of two distinct PIE etyma (Vedic *ahi-* / Avestan *aži-* on the one hand, Hittite *illuyanka-* on the other), with the lexical-substitutability of the noun-slot being one of Watkins's points about the formula's structure rather than its words.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the philological structure of the formula as verb-slot + object-slot with the verb-slot showing tighter cross-language lexical correspondence than the object-slot, and on the formula as a structure-of-slots rather than a fixed phrase; per the brief's structure section I on the subject + verb-of-killing + serpent-object framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (formula as structure-of-slots)

The formula's distinctive feature, on Watkins's reading, is that it is a *poetic* inheritance — preserved in the verbal diction of poets composing in successive IE languages — rather than a *narrative* inheritance, a *theological* inheritance, or a *ritual* inheritance: what the formula transmits across the daughter languages is the verbal way of saying the combat, not necessarily the same theology of the combat or the same ritual occasion at which the combat is recited.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters on the formula as poetic-verbal inheritance distinct from narrative, theological, or ritual inheritance; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml comparative_argument on the formula as Indo-European poetic tradition rather than as a structural-typological category.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (poetic-verbal inheritance)

This poetic-verbal framing is methodologically distinct from a Frazerian structural-typological comparativism (which would group dragon-killings by structural-functional similarity without making language-specific claims) and from a Dumézilian functional-tripartite comparativism (which would group dragon-slayer figures by their position in the IE warrior-function), and Watkins's argument is that the lexical-syntactic correspondences carry an evidential weight that purely structural-or-functional comparisons cannot.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters on the philological-comparative method's distinctness from Frazerian and Dumézilian comparativisms and on the lexical-syntactic correspondences as evidentially distinct from structural-or-functional resemblances; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml on the methodological-distinctness framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters (philological vs. structural method)

The chronological depth of the reconstruction depends on the early-attestation status of Hittite within the IE family. Hittite is the earliest-attested IE language (cuneiform tablets from the Hattusa archives c. 1700–1180 BCE) and is conventionally regarded as an early-branching member of the family; the presence of the formula in Hittite — alongside its Vedic and Avestan reflexes attested c. 1500–500 BCE — implies that the formula was already in place at a stage of the family that predates the divergence of the Anatolian and non-Anatolian branches.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 and methodological framing chapters on the Hittite reflex's chronological significance for dating the reconstructed formula; on Hittite's status as the earliest-attested IE language and as an early-branching member of the family; per glossary.yaml on "Proto-Indo-European" first-mention gloss "the reconstructed common ancestor of the Indo-European language family, spoken c. 4500–2500 BCE; Watkins's HERO SLAY SERPENT formula is a reconstructed PIE poetic element".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (chronological depth and Hittite early-branching)

The Norse-skaldic kennings for the serpent (the compressed metaphorical phrases in Old Norse / Old English skaldic poetic diction — for example, *umbgjǫrð allra landa* "band of all lands" for Jörmungandr) preserve, on Watkins's reading in *How to Kill a Dragon* Part V, the IE serpent-combat vocabulary in a registered-poetic-diction form that supplies the chapter's principal post-Vedic, post-Anatolian extra-philological exhibit for the formula's continued life in the daughter-language poetic traditions.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the Norse skaldic kennings for Jörmungandr and on the kennings as preserving the IE serpent-combat vocabulary in registered-poetic-diction form; per glossary.yaml on "kenning" first-mention gloss "an Old Norse / Old English compressed metaphorical phrase; kennings for Jörmungandr (e.g., 'band of all lands') preserve the IE serpent-combat vocabulary that Watkins analyses".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Norse skaldic kennings)

West 2007 ch. 6 ("Storm and Stream") supplements Watkins's reconstruction with a wider Indo-European storm-god / water-serpent complex, in which the formula's verbal exhibits sit alongside structural-typological observations about the storm-god's function as combatant and the water-serpent's function as obstructor; the West 2007 framing supplies the chapter's principal IE-comparative apparatus for the storm-god structural pattern alongside Watkins's verbal-formulaic apparatus.footnote:[West, M.L., *Indo-European Poetry and Myth* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2007), ch. 6 ("Storm and Stream"), on the IE storm-god / water-serpent complex and on the structural-typological framing that supplements Watkins's verbal-formulaic apparatus; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml sources.anchor on West 2007 as a chapter anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Storm and Stream)

The formula's evidential profile within the IE family is therefore strongest where the verb-and-object pairing is preserved in attested poetic-or-narrative text in a daughter language with an early or otherwise high-prestige textual record (Vedic, Avestan, Hittite, Old Norse, Old English) and weakest where the verb-and-object pairing is reconstructed from indirect or fragmentary witnesses (Slavic, Armenian) or where the philological signal is replaced by a typological-structural signal in the absence of preserved formulaic diction (Celtic, Welsh, in part Greek).footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the gradient of evidential strength across the daughter-language reflexes; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section IV on the strong / weak distinction between Vedic-Avestan-Hittite direct philological correspondence and Celtic / Slavic typological-or-reconstructed evidence; West 2007 ch. 6 on the same gradient at the level of the storm-god structural pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (evidential gradient)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (storm-god structural pattern across IE)

[INFERENCE: the chapter's framing of Watkins's argument as primarily a *philological* inheritance argument — distinct from structural-typological or theological inheritance arguments — is the chapter's editorial reading of the methodological framing chapters of Watkins 1995, presented in the present chapter as the principal interpretive lens for §§II–IV; Watkins's volume is itself titled *Aspects of Indo-European Poetics* and the verbal-formulaic emphasis is consonant with the volume's self-presentation, but the framing language used in the present chapter ("philological-genealogical inheritance distinct from structural-typological resemblance") is a chapter-level synthesis rather than a Watkins-1995 quotation. | basis: Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters and the volume's title ("Aspects of Indo-European Poetics"); brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml comparative_argument on the chapter's framing of the formula as IE poetic inheritance; per the chapter-claims skill discipline that requires editorial framing-language to be flagged where it tightens or interprets a source's argument | risk: alternative readings of Watkins's volume could foreground the structural or the typological dimensions; the chapter's philological-emphasis framing is one reasonable reading but not the only available reading.]

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch14 (Indra-Vṛtra) — back-link to the Vedic-side anchor (Jamison & Brereton 2014 on RV 1.32 and the supplementary hymn-cycle; Benveniste & Renou 1934 on the Vedic-Avestan philological core); §I above introduces the Vedic *áhann áhim* phrasing as the formula's reflex anchor and §II below develops the Vedic case in more detail.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch6 (Illuyanka) — back-link to the Hittite-side anchor (Hoffner 1998 working English translation of CTH 321; Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25 *editio princeps*); the Watkins formula material is treated more briefly in Ch6 (which is principally a *purulli*-festival chapter — see Ch36 §III) and the present chapter's §II develops the Hittite philological case as one of the three direct cases.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch33 (Methods) — back-link to the methods sidebar's discussion of philological-comparative method, in which Watkins's lexical-syntactic-correspondence method is contrasted with structural-typological and functional-comparative methods; the present chapter's §I framing of the formula as philological-genealogical inheritance is the comparative-chapter realisation of the methods sidebar's distinctions.


=== II. The direct philological cases

The second body section develops the three direct philological cases at which Watkins's reconstruction is anchored on regular cross-language lexical-and-syntactic correspondences rather than on typological reading.

The §II direct philological cases are the three IE daughter-language reflexes — Vedic Indra-Vṛtra, Avestan Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka, and Hittite Tarḫunna-Illuyanka — at which Watkins's reconstructed formula is anchored on regular lexical-and-syntactic correspondences across attested-text witnesses, and at which the chapter's case for Indo-European inheritance does not depend on typological reading or on reconstruction from indirect evidence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the Vedic-Avestan-Hittite triple as the philologically-direct anchor of the reconstructed formula; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section II on the three-direct-case framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (Vedic / Avestan / Hittite as direct anchor)

The Vedic case is anchored on Rigveda 1.32, the principal Indra-Vṛtra hymn (fifteen verses), whose second verse contains *áhann áhim*, "he slew the serpent" — the formula's most-cited lexical exhibit; the chapter cites RV 1.32 as the working text via Jamison and Brereton 2014, the scholarly-standard English Rigveda, and reads the *áhann áhim* phrase at 1.32.2 as the Vedic instantiation of the verb-and-object pairing that Watkins reconstructs as the formula.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on RV 1.32 as the principal Indra-Vṛtra hymn and on the *áhann áhim* phrasing at 1.32.2 (the second verse, with the opening verse announcing the theme of Indra's manly deeds); Watkins 1995, ch. 46 (and broader Part V), on the same line as the Vedic reflex anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 1.32 (and 1.32.1-2)

The Vedic Vṛtra-corpus extends well beyond RV 1.32: at least eight Rigvedic hymns (RV 1.32; 1.80; 2.11; 2.12; 4.18; 5.32; 6.17; 8.96; 10.113) constitute the working Vṛtra-combat corpus on which Watkins's reading of the Vedic reflex draws, with RV 1.32 supplying the most concentrated narrative spine and the other seven hymns supplying co-witness motifs that the spine-text does not develop, and the formula recurs across the corpus rather than being a one-line feature of a single hymn.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the eight Vṛtra-hymns as the working Vṛtra-combat corpus; brief 14-indra-vrtra.yaml sources.primary on the same eight hymns; Watkins 1995, chs. 41–48, on the recurrence of the formula across the Vṛtra-corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 1.32; 1.80; 2.11-12; 4.18; 5.32; 6.17; 8.96; 10.113
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=chs. 41-48 (Vedic Vṛtra-corpus)

The Vedic combat is liturgically embedded: the Rigveda is a corpus of ritual hymns recited at the *soma* sacrifice, and the *áhann áhim* phrasing appears in hymns whose ritual function is to invoke the *soma*-strengthened Indra in the present petition, so that every recitation of RV 1.32 in its ritual context re-actualises the formula in performance — a feature that distinguishes the Vedic reflex from the Hittite reflex (where the formula appears in a one-time CTH 321 narrative rather than in a daily-or-frequent ritual hymn).footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the Rigveda's liturgical character and on the ritual-recitation context of the Vṛtra-hymns; per brief 14-indra-vrtra.yaml on the *soma*-sacrifice context of the Vṛtra-hymns; Watkins 1995, on the Vedic reflex's poetic-liturgical character.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=Vṛtra-hymns (liturgical-ritual context)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Vedic poetic-liturgical context)

The Vedic reflex is reinforced by Trita Āptya, the older Vedic dragon-slayer whose name (Vedic *Trita*, "third") is philologically cognate with Avestan Thraētaona by regular Indo-Iranian sound-laws (Vedic *tr-* / Avestan *thr-*; Vedic *-i-* / Avestan *-aē-*; Vedic *-a* / Avestan *-aona*), and whose attestation in the Rigvedic corpus (RV 10.99.6; 1.105; 2.11.19–20) preserves a pre-Indra Indo-Iranian stratum of the dragon-slayer pattern.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Trita / Thraētaona phonological cognancy and on the pre-Indra Indo-Iranian stratum; Jamison and Brereton 2014, on RV 10.99.6 and 1.105 and 2.11.19–20 as the Trita Āptya hymn-cluster; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–45, on the Trita / Thraētaona pair as a load-bearing Indo-Iranian exhibit.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita-Thraētaona discussion
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 10.99.6; 1.105; 2.11.19-20

The Trita / Thraētaona cognancy is documented at two levels — at the level of the names themselves (the regular sound-law correspondences across the Indo-Iranian branches) and at the level of their adversaries (both figures slay a three-headed serpent, the Vedic adversary unnamed in the surviving hymn-allusions and the Avestan adversary named as Aži Dahāka, "the dragon-Dahāka") — and the pair is one of the load-bearing exhibits for an Indo-Iranian phase of the dragon-combat material before the divergence of the Indic and Iranian branches.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the lexical-and-structural Trita / Thraētaona cognancy as evidence for an Indo-Iranian stratum; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–45, on the same evidential function; per brief 15-vedic-reflexes.yaml on the philologically-load-bearing status of the pair.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita-Thraētaona discussion (Indo-Iranian phase)

The Avestan case is anchored on the Avestan yashts (Yt. 5.33–35, in which Thraētaona prays to Anāhitā for victory over Aži Dahāka; Yt. 14, in which Vərəθraγna's smashing of resistance is named; Yt. 19, the Frashokereti material on the eschatological combat) and on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn*, in which the Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka combat is preserved as a continuous narrative; Benveniste and Renou 1934 supply the standard philological apparatus integrating both layers.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Avestan yasht material (Yt. 5.33–35; Yt. 14; Yt. 19) and on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* narrative; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–48, on the Avestan reflexes within the consolidated IE formula reconstruction; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml on the Avestan-and-Pahlavi witness layers.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Yt. 5.33-35; Yt. 14; Yt. 19; Bundahišn material
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V chs. 44-48 (Avestan reflexes)

The Avestan reflex is hymn-allusive rather than narrative: the surviving Avestan corpus does not preserve a continuous narrative of the Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka combat, and the yasht-allusions — Yt. 5.33–35 in particular, where Thraētaona prays for the strength to slay the three-mouthed, three-headed, six-eyed Aži Dahāka — supply only the verbal-and-prayer-context attestations of the combat at the level of formulaic invocation rather than at the level of full storytelling.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the hymn-allusive character of the Avestan attestations and on Yt. 5.33–35 specifically; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae on the absence of a continuous Avestan narrative and on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* as the fullest narrative witness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Yt. 5.33-35 (hymn-allusive prayer-context)

The Pahlavi *Bundahišn* — a Middle Persian compilation conventionally dated to the 9th century CE in its surviving redaction, drawing on earlier Sasanian-period materials — supplies the fullest narrative witness to the Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka combat (the binding of the dragon under Mount Damāvand) and to the eschatological pendant in which Aži Dahāka escapes and is finally killed by the revived Kərəsāspa at Frashokereti; the Pahlavi witness is post-Achaemenid and must be marked as such in any Indo-European-comparative argument that draws on it.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* as the fullest narrative witness and on the binding-and-final-killing structure; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–48, on the Pahlavi material's use within the consolidated IE reconstruction; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae on the post-Achaemenid status of the Pahlavi witness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Pahlavi Bundahišn (continuous narrative witness)

[LACUNA: the Avestan corpus preserves no continuous narrative of the Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka combat; the yasht-allusions are hymn-formulaic-and-prayer-context, and the continuous-narrative witness is the post-Achaemenid Pahlavi *Bundahišn*. | source: per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml lacunae and per the Avestan yasht corpus as preserved in standard editions; Benveniste and Renou 1934 on the hymn-allusive character of the Avestan attestations | scholarly_reconstruction: Benveniste and Renou 1934 reconstruct the combat-narrative by reading the Avestan yasht-allusions alongside the Pahlavi *Bundahišn* narrative, with the Pahlavi-witness narrative read as the continuation-and-elaboration of the Avestan-attested combat-tradition; the chapter cites the Pahlavi material as the in-tradition fill rather than as an Avestan-period attestation.]

The Avestan adversary Aži Dahāka is morphologically three-headed (Avestan: three-mouthed, six-eyed, three-jawed), and the three-headed serpent / dragon morphology is precisely the morphology of the Vedic adversary slain by Trita Āptya in RV 10.99.6 — read by Benveniste and Renou and by Watkins as the Indo-Iranian common adversary of the Trita / Thraētaona cognate pair, with the cognancy of the names confirming the cognancy of the combat-narratives.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the three-headed-adversary cognancy across Trita / Aži Dahāka and Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka; Watkins 1995, Part V chs. 44–45, on the structural-and-philological cognancy of the Indo-Iranian dragon-slayer pair; Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the three-headed-adversary attestation at RV 10.99.6.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Trita-Thraētaona / three-headed-adversary discussion
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 10.99.6 (three-headed adversary)

The Hittite case — the chapter renders the storm-god as Tarḫunna in keeping with the Ch36 §III correction, since Tarḫunna is the Hittite-language name of the storm-god in CTH 321 and Teshub is the Hurrian-language name attested in the Kumarbi Cycle and Hurrian-language ritual material — is anchored on CTH 321 (the Illuyanka tablets from the Hattusa archives), preserved in two co-equal scribal versions on the same tablet group, with both versions framed by their colophons as the recitation belonging to the *purulli* festival of the storm-god of Nerik.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, *Hittite Myths*, "Illuyanka" §§1–2 with framing colophons; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25, on the *editio princeps* and the two-version structure; per glossary.yaml on "Tarḫunna / Teshub" with Tarḫunna in Hittite-language contexts and Teshub in Hurrian-language contexts.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2 with framing colophons
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25

The Hittite verb-and-object pairing *kuenta… illuyanka-* — "he slew… the serpent" — is the chapter's principal Hittite philological exhibit: the verb *kuen-* (third-singular preterite *kuenta*) is the Hittite reflex of the PIE killing-verb *gʷhen-* whose Vedic and Avestan reflexes (*han-* / *jan-*) anchor the formula in Indo-Iranian, and *illuyanka-* is the Hittite common-noun "serpent" used as the proper name of the storm-god's adversary.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 ("Typhoeus and the Illuyankas"), on the *kuenta… illuyanka-* verb-and-object pairing as the Hittite reflex of the IE formula; Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2, on the Hittite text containing the pairing; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25, on the *editio princeps*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite cognate formulation)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2

The two Illuyanka versions are co-equally attested on the same CTH 321 tablet group: Version 1 (Inara prepares a banquet, Hupasiya binds the gorged serpent, the storm-god kills the bound Illuyanka) and Version 2 (Illuyanka takes the storm-god's heart and eyes, the storm-god's half-mortal son recovers the organs as bride-price, the storm-god kills both Illuyanka and his own son in renewed combat at the sea); Beckman's *editio princeps* establishes the two versions as scribally co-equal, and the chapter cites both as carrying the Hittite philological reflex of the formula at the level of the verb-and-object pairing whose common cross-version status reinforces the philological signal.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2 on the two versions; Beckman 1982, *JANES* 14: 11–25 on the scribal co-equality of the two versions; per brief 06-illuyanka.yaml variants.classification on co-equal status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2 (two co-equal versions)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25 (scribal co-equality)

[LACUNA: the precise distribution of the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing across the two CTH 321 versions is not fully reconstructable in every preserved instance because the Hittite tablets themselves are partially broken (Version 1 closing section partially broken; Version 2 mid-narrative transitions partially broken per Hoffner 1998). | source: per brief 06-illuyanka.yaml lacunae on the broken closing section of Version 1 and the broken mid-narrative transitions of Version 2; Hoffner 1998 *Hittite Myths* on the textual breaks | scholarly_reconstruction: Watkins 1995 ch. 46 reads the formula as preserved in CTH 321 at the level of the verb-and-object pairing without depending on the breaks; Hoffner 1998 supplies the reconstructed text at the level of the surrounding narrative, and Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25 supplies the philological *editio princeps* against which the formula's Hittite-language realisations are checked.]

The Hittite reflex's chronological significance for the reconstruction is decisive: Hittite is conventionally regarded as an early-branching member of the IE family (the "Anatolian hypothesis" reading Anatolian as the first branch to separate; the "Indo-Hittite hypothesis" reading Anatolian as a sister-language to the rest of IE rather than a daughter), and the presence of the formula in CTH 321 alongside its Vedic and Avestan reflexes implies that the formula was already established at a stage of the family that predates the Anatolian / non-Anatolian divergence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 and methodological framing chapters, on the Hittite reflex's chronological significance; on the Anatolian-early-branching status of Hittite within the IE family; per glossary.yaml on "Proto-Indo-European" as the reconstructed common ancestor c. 4500–2500 BCE.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 (Hittite chronological significance)

The three direct cases together — Vedic, Avestan, Hittite — supply philological evidence at three independent points in the IE family: Indo-Iranian (Vedic and Avestan as cognate witnesses to a shared Indo-Iranian phase of the formula) and Anatolian (Hittite as an independent early-branching witness), with the three-witness pattern crossing the Anatolian / non-Anatolian boundary and the Indo-Iranian internal boundary so that the formula's Indo-European depth is attested rather than reconstructed across at least two principal branches of the family.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the three-witness Vedic-Avestan-Hittite cross-branch attestation as the chapter's philological-evidential floor; Benveniste and Renou 1934, on the Indo-Iranian internal-branch witnessing; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section II on the Vedic / Avestan / Hittite triple as the direct philological case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (cross-branch attestation)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=Indo-Iranian internal-branch witnessing

The §II direct-cases argument's load-bearing claim is therefore that the formula's existence as an Indo-European inheritance is attested in three IE daughter languages on the strength of regular phonological correspondences between cognate verbs and cognate nouns, that the inheritance reading does not depend on speculative reconstruction or on typological resemblance, and that the §III extended cases — to which the chapter now turns — are best read as later-branching reflexes of the same inheritance whose evidential character varies along the gradient that §I and §IV develop.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the philological-attestation status of the §II direct cases and on the extended-cases gradient; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section II / III / IV on the three-direct-case status and on the gradient framing of the extended cases; per the Ch34 / Ch35 comparative-chapter precedent on the load-bearing-claim framing for §II material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (load-bearing direct-cases claim)

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch14 (Indra-Vṛtra) and Ch15 (Vedic reflexes) — back-link to the Vedic-side anchors (Jamison & Brereton 2014 on RV 1.32 and the supplementary hymn-cycle; Benveniste & Renou 1934 on the Trita / Thraētaona Indo-Iranian pair); §II above develops the Vedic case as the formula's most-cited reflex anchor and the Trita Āptya material as the pre-Indra Indo-Iranian stratum within the Vedic-side evidence.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch13 (Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka) — back-link to the Avestan-side anchor (Benveniste & Renou 1934 on the Avestan yashts and the Pahlavi *Bundahišn*; Watkins 1995 Part V chs. 44–48 on the Avestan reflexes); §II above develops the Avestan case with explicit hymn-allusive vs. continuous-narrative distinction between the Avestan and Pahlavi witnesses, and flags the Pahlavi-narrative status as post-Achaemenid in-tradition fill.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Ch6 (Illuyanka) and Ch36 §III (cyclical-maintenance) — back-link to the Hittite-side anchors (Hoffner 1998 *Hittite Myths* working English translation of CTH 321; Beckman 1982 *JANES* 14: 11–25 *editio princeps*; Watkins 1995 ch. 46 on the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing); the chapter renders the storm-god as Tarḫunna in keeping with the Ch36 §III locked correction (Hittite-language storm-god of CTH 321) rather than as Teshub (the Hurrian-language form attested in the Kumarbi Cycle).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C4. Eschatological displacement (Ch37) — the Avestan / Pahlavi Frashokereti material on Aži Dahāka's binding-and-final-killing is treated at length in C4 as the eschatological-subtype Iranian case; the present chapter's §II treatment of the Avestan witness is restricted to the philological reflex of the formula at the level of the verb-and-object pairing and does not develop the eschatological reading that C4 develops.


=== III. The extended typological cases

The third body section consolidates the eight extended typological cases on a philological-evidence spectrum from preserved formulaic diction to fragmentary or contested reconstruction.

The §III extended typological cases are the IE daughter-language reflexes — Greek Zeus-Typhon; Norse Thor-Jörmungandr and Sigurd-Fáfnir; Old English *Beowulf*-dragon; Celtic Lugh-Balor; Welsh Red-and-White Dragon; Ossetic Batraz; Armenian Vahagn-vishaps; Slavic Perun-Veles and Dobrynya-Zmey-Gorynych — at which Watkins's reconstructed formula is anchored in some cases on preserved poetic-formulaic diction (Norse Sigurd; Old English *Beowulf*) and in other cases on structural-typological resemblance, fragmentary attestation, or contested reconstruction; the §III cases are arranged here on a philological-evidence spectrum from strongest (Norse skaldic and Eddic; *Beowulf*) to weakest (Slavic Perun-Veles, Armenian Vahagn).footnote:[Watkins 1995, Parts IV–V, on the §III extended-cases gradient; per brief 38-C5-ie-hero-slay-serpent-formula.yaml structure section III on the Greek / Norse / Germanic / Celtic / Welsh / Ossetic / Armenian / Slavic case-list; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the philological-evidence-spectrum ordering of the family-chapter sub-cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Parts IV-V (extended-cases gradient)

The Greek Zeus-Typhon combat is the Hellenic instance of the IE warrior-and-serpent pattern: at Hesiod's *Theogony* 820–880 (the chapter's working text via West 1966), Zeus defeats the hundred-headed, serpent-limbed Typhon, the earth-born monster generated by Gaia and Tartarus, in the closing combat of the Olympian succession; the Typhonomachy is structurally the Greek sovereignty-establishing combat that secures Zeus's kingship over gods and men and is the principal Greek exhibit for the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern in Hesiodic poetic-narrative form.footnote:[West 1966, *Hesiod: Theogony*, on Theog. 820–880 as the closing combat of the Olympian succession and on the structural function of the Typhonomachy; Ogden 2013, *Drakōn*, chs. 2–3, on the Greek combat-tradition and on Typhon as the principal Greek instance; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the Hesiod-and-later-tradition framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1966-theogony ; loc=Theog. 820-880
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=chs. 2-3

The Greek-Hittite philological connection in the Zeus-Typhon material is contested rather than consolidated: West 1997 and Burkert 1992 read the Hesiodic succession (Ouranos-Kronos-Zeus) and the Typhonomachy as derived from the Kumarbi Cycle and (less directly) from the Illuyanka tradition via Near Eastern mediation, but this transmission-reading is the C6 chapter's territory rather than the present chapter's, and the present chapter treats Zeus-Typhon as a typological IE instance whose relation to the Hittite material is inheritance-or-contact rather than a direct philological cognate of the *kuenta… illuyanka-* pairing.footnote:[West 1997, *East Face of Helicon*, on the Kumarbi-Hesiod transmission reading; Burkert 1992, *Orientalizing Revolution*, on the Levantine transmission of combat-myth into Greek; per brief 11-zeus-typhon.yaml on the Near-Eastern-transmission framing; per the C6 (Ch39) chapter on the transmission question that the present chapter does not adjudicate.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=East Face of Helicon (Kumarbi-Hesiod transmission)

The Norse case has two principal sub-instances within the IE-formula argument. The first is Thor against Jörmungandr — the world-encircling Midgard Serpent — preserved in three encounters across the Old Norse corpus (Útgarðaloki's lifting-of-the-cat in Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 46–48; the fishing expedition with Hymir in *Hymiskviða* and Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 48 with Húsdrápa as skaldic witness; the mutual killing at Ragnarök in *Völuspá* 56 and Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 51), and Watkins reads the skaldic kennings for the serpent (*umbgjǫrð allra landa* and similar phrases) as preserving the IE serpent-combat vocabulary at the level of the registered-poetic-diction kenning.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *The Poetic Edda*, on *Hymiskviða* and *Völuspá* 56; Faulkes 1987, on *Gylfaginning* 34, 46–48, and 51 and on the Húsdrápa skaldic citations; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the skaldic kennings and on the IE serpent-combat vocabulary; per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the three-encounter structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Hymiskviða; Völuspá 56
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 34, 46-48, 51
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Norse skaldic kennings)

The second Norse sub-instance is Sigurd against Fáfnir, preserved principally in *Fáfnismál* in the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE), with parallel witnesses in the *Völsunga saga* (c. 1270 CE) and in the Middle High German *Nibelungenlied* (c. 1200 CE) as the related Germanic witness with Sigurd in the German form Siegfried; Watkins's chapter 42 on Sigurd is among the strongest philological cases in the post-Indo-Iranian, post-Anatolian corpus, with the Old Norse verb of killing (*vega*, "to kill in combat") and the named hero-and-named-dragon pairing instantiating the IE formula in Old Norse.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems; Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the *Fáfnismál* diction of Sigurd's killing as a direct reflex of the reconstructed IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on Sigurd as the strongest philological evidence in the family chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42 (Sigurd / Fáfnir formula reflex)

The Old English *Beowulf* preserves the formula in the third movement of the poem (lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering), in which the aged Beowulf confronts and kills the treasure-hoarding *draca* and dies of the dragon's bite; Watkins reads the Old English dragon-vocabulary (*draca*, *wyrm*) and the killing-vocabulary as instantiating the IE formula in Old English, and Tolkien's 1936 British Academy lecture supplies the load-bearing critical move that re-established the mythic register of the *Beowulf* monsters within twentieth-century Old English scholarship.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the *Beowulf* dragon-episode as IE HERO SLAY SERPENT reflex in the killing-diction and the narrative-structure; Tolkien, J.R.R., "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics", *Proc. British Academy* 22 (1936): 245–295, on the dragon as proper mythic adversary integral to the poem's structure; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the convergence of the philological and the structural-critical arguments.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (Beowulf formula reflex)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proc. British Academy 22 (1936)

The *Beowulf* case is also distinctive in that the dragon-vocabulary itself shows IE depth: the Old English *draca* (loaned from Latin *draco* but ultimately tracing to the same Greek-and-Indo-European stem from which Greek *drakōn* derives) and the inherited Germanic *wyrm* (from Proto-Germanic *wurmiz*, ultimately from PIE *wr̥mi-* "worm/serpent") supply two etymologically-distinct lexical exhibits for the serpent-object slot of the IE formula in a single Old English poem, and the doubled-vocabulary attestation reinforces the IE reflex-reading at the level of inherited word-stock rather than only at the level of preserved formulaic diction.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the Old English *draca* / *wyrm* doubled-vocabulary attestation as IE-formula reflex; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the *draca* / *wyrm* lexical pairing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V (draca / wyrm doubled vocabulary)

The Celtic case is Lugh against Balor at the Second Battle of Mag Tuired, preserved in the Middle Irish prose narrative *Cath Maige Tuired* (manuscripts 12th–16th c. CE): Lugh of the Tuatha Dé Danann kills his evil-eyed Fomorian grandfather Balor with a sling-stone that drives the eye through the back of Balor's head and turns its destructive gaze on the Fomorian host behind him; West 2007 ch. 6 reads the Celtic case as the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern, with the morphological distinctiveness preserved (Balor is a one-eyed giant rather than a serpent, and the killing-weapon is a projectile rather than a sword or thunderbolt).footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the Celtic Lugh-Balor case as the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern with morphological distinctiveness; Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the Cath Maige Tuired tradition; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Lugh-Balor sub-case; *Cath Maige Tuired* edition (Gray 1982 Irish Texts Society) named-but-flagged for inventory-audit follow-up at glossary-lock pinning.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Celtic Lugh-Balor)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The Lugh-Balor case's evidential weight for the IE formula argument is therefore principally typological and dynastic-structural: Lugh's hybrid lineage (Tuatha Dé father / Fomorian mother / Fomorian grandfather Balor) supplies the kill-the-grandfather motif that West 2007 ch. 6 reads as the IE warrior structural-genealogical pattern, but the philological reflex of *gʷhen-* in the Old Irish killing-vocabulary is not the focal exhibit, and the case's contribution to the consolidated IE argument is at the level of structural pattern rather than at the level of lexical cognancy.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6, on the kill-the-grandfather motif as the IE warrior structural-genealogical pattern in the Celtic case; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the typological-rather-than-philological character of the Celtic Lugh-Balor evidential contribution.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Celtic typological-structural contribution)

The Welsh Red-and-White Dragon case is preserved in two principal witnesses with distinct framings — the Latin *Historia Brittonum* (Nennius recension, c. 828 CE) presenting the Britons-vs-Saxons political allegory, and the Middle Welsh *Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion* (manuscripts White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE; Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE) preserving an older non-political reading in which the combat is an annual May-Eve disturbance ended by the king Lludd's mead-pit-and-burial resolution at Dinas Emrys — and the Welsh case is structurally distinctive in the IE-reflex corpus because the combat is between two dragons rather than between a hero and a dragon.footnote:[Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the *Historia Brittonum* and *Lludd and Llefelys* recensions and on the dragon-on-dragon combat structure; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Welsh case's structural distinctiveness within the IE-reflex corpus and on the two-witness side-by-side presentation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (Welsh dragon-on-dragon as IE comparative anchor)

The Welsh case is also distinctive in its preservation of an annual-disturbance reading (in *Lludd and Llefelys*) alongside the political-allegorical reading (in *Historia Brittonum*): the May-Eve recurrence of the dragon-combat in *Lludd and Llefelys* shares structural affinity with the Hittite *purulli* annual cycle (treated in C3 / Ch36 §III) and with the Apollo-Python Septerion at Delphi (also a cyclical-cult-foundation reflex), and the Welsh dual recension preserves both the political-prophetic and the cyclical-cosmological dimensions of a single dragon-tradition in two contemporaneous witnesses that the medieval Welsh literary culture transmitted side by side.footnote:[Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material, on the *Lludd and Llefelys* annual-disturbance reading and on the Welsh dual-recension preservation; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the older-substrate reading of *Lludd and Llefelys*; per the C3 (Ch36 §III) framing on the *purulli* and Septerion cyclical-maintenance reflexes for comparative context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material (Welsh dual recension)
